Approval
This thesis has been prepared over five months in the Department of Environmental and Resource
Engineering, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfillment of the Master of
Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. in Civil Engineering.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge of terminology and concepts relating to Geotech-
nics and Soil Mechanics.

ii
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The increasing development of offshore wind farms increases the importance of describing the
non-linear shear modulus degradation, as well as the small strain shear modulus G0 in sands. This
is particularly the case for monopile foundation design. This thesis investigates methods for obtain-
ing reliable and repeatable shear degradation curves in sand, utilizing several test types. These tests
include the bender element test, resonant column test, triaxial test with local strain measurements,
and direct simple shear test. An extensive laboratory program was conducted, which showed
the significant importance of high precision testing, for obtaining and interpreting shear modu-
lus degradation in sand. By combining the shear modulus degradation curves of all tests, it was
possible to construct full shear degradation curves, however, with some uncertainty and deviations.
The bender elements received particular attention, due to their widespread use and oversimplified
interpretation methods. Here, the widely used time domain interpretation methods first arrival and
peak to peak were evaluated. A more recent interpretation method using a finite element solution in
the software ”GeoHyTE” was evaluated. GeoHyTE proved to show objective and reliable interpre-
tations. The minimal user interpretation needed in GeoHyTE ensured the exclusion of subjective
interpretations associated with the first arrival and peak to peak method. Furthermore, GeoHyTE
effectively addressed uncertainties caused by the near-field effects, a challenge that conventional
time domain interpretation methods are not able to resolve. Comparison of different signal types
was conducted, where it was found that sinusoidal burst signals were the most reliable, compared
to ramp and square bust signals. The study identified potential correlations between the shear
wave velocity Vs, and different soil parameters, such as specimen saturation, maximum angle of
dilatancy ψmax, and the critical state parameter Ψ. Furthermore, unloading/reloading in the shear
phase of the triaxial tests showed values of the Poisson’s ratio νUR, which were higher than those
obtained from calculation.

Acknowledgements
This project represents the final requirement for completing the Civil Engineering Master of Sci-
ence program at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The project was carried out in col-
laboration with Geo Copenhagen and DTU, from 26th of August 2024 to 26th of January 2025. The
project was completed under supervision by Abdalla Mustafa Abdalla Almukashfi, Geotechnical
Engineer (Geo) and Irene Rocchi, Associate Professor (DTU Sustain).
I would first and foremost like to show my gratitude to my supervisors, Abdalla Almukashfi and
Irene Rocchi, for their valuable knowledge and guidance throughout this thesis. Without their
insight throughout the project, the thesis would have never been completed.
Furthermore, I would like to thank Frederik Peter Ditlevsen for allowing me to use the laboratory
equipment at Geo, making it possible to conduct the needed experiments to finish the project.
In addition, I would like to show my gratitude to Ionuţ Dragoş Moldovan and the Technical Uni-
versity of Lisbon, for providing the GeoHyTE software, which was a huge part of the thesis.
A huge thank you to Anders Hust Augustesen and Ørsted for providing the sand used in the exper-
imental tests during the thesis. Furthermore, I would like to thank Anders Hust Augustesen and
Christopher Gerlach for their input on the experimental phase.
I would like to thank my colleagues in the engineering team at Geo, Ertol Zhurda, Jeppe Bendix
Regel, Ole Jørgensen, Svend Pilgaard Larsen and Tobias Orlander for their guidance on project
management, physics, preparation, and interpretation in experimental testing.
I would like to thank the current and former technicians at Geo, Daniel Meyer, Daniel Mundt
Andersen, Emil Jønsson, Lars Rahbek Christoffersen, Niels Weidick and Umar Uzdenov, for their
guidance in equipment usage, as well as preparing equipment.
Overall, I am thankful for all the support I have received from my colleagues at Geo, which greatly
helped to finish this thesis.
In addition, I would like to thank Dagmar Tørrild Thorbek for providing feedback on the project.
Finally, as I complete my studies at DTU, I would like to thank my family for the support they
have shown up to this point.

Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
With the increasing development of offshore wind farms, the importance of non-linear shear mod-
ulus degradation in sands has grown. In 2013 the Pile Soil Analysis (PISA) project started, with
Ørsted leading the project (The Carbon Trust, 2025). The PISA project investigated the interaction
between soil conditions and monopiles, which are often used in offshore wind farm foundations.
The new design method derived from this project had the potential to reduce the steel requirements
in monopiles by up to 30 % under certain conditions. The PISA framework uses the small strain
shear modulus G0 as a key input parameter. This modulus is obtainable from the shear wave ve-
locity Vs found in bender element (BE) tests in the soil, as well as the resonant column (RC) test.
G0 is found by the shear wave velocity Vs, which can be determined by the bender element (BE)
test and the resonant column (RC) test.
The RC test is widely used to obtain the shear modulus G, in small strain ranges. However, a
disadvantage of the RC test is the cost and complexity of the test setup. Therefore, BEs have been
used as an alternative to the RC test to obtain G0 in the soil, due to their ease of use (Almukashfi
et al., 2024). However, BE testing still contains many uncertainties in the interpretation of test
results, which need to be assessed (Moldovan et al., 2024). The main issue of BE tests is the
determination of the wave travel times. Prior research has utilized time and frequency domain
interpretation methods, however, without clear agreement (Almukashfi et al., 2024). For example,
the ASTM standard (ASTM International, 2019) suggests ”start to start” and ”peak to peak” in
the time domain, without a statement on the precision of these methods. This becomes an issue
when operators use these methods as subjectivity and bias is introduced. Arroyo et al. (2010)
reported that they received the same BE signal for the interpretation of the arrival time using the
first break. In this investigation, the uncertainty in determining G0 was found to be 10 % to 20 %
between several interpreters. Similarly, Ogino et al. (2015) observed a deviation of up to 40 % in
G0 between the frequency domain and the time domain interpretation methods.
In this thesis, an extensive laboratory program was designed to describe shear modulus degradation
in sand, from very small strain ranges to larger strain ranges. Here, BE and RC tests were used in
small strain ranges, triaxial tests with local strain measurements were used in intermediate strain
ranges, and direct simple shear (DSS) tests were used in larger strain ranges. Combining these test
methods, full shear degradation curves were obtained to describe the behavior of the sand at all
strain ranges. Furthermore, soil parameters were obtained from the triaxial test, including the angle
of dilatancy, state parameter, stiffness, and Poisson’s ratio. These were used to assess correlation
with Vs, as well as to describe the behavior of the soil.
The goal of the thesis includes the presentation of improved methods for measuring and inter-
preting stiffness parameters. This includes not only interpretation methods but also methods for
conducting tests to obtain reliable and repeatable test results. A major focus is on BE testing using
methods that eliminate subjective interpretation. This is achieved by comparison and improvement
of current subjective interpretation methods, as well as comparison with recently developed inter-
pretation methods. These methods include the finite element (FE) interpretation software ”Geo-
HyTE”, which requires little to no user intervention for interpretation of BE results. Furthermore,
a full interpretation of the state, strength, and stiffness parameters of the soil was investigated, as
these are utilized in the constitutive models to describe soil behavior. The thesis therefore aims to
answer the following study questions:
• How can reliable and repeatable shear degradation curves be obtained?
• Which BE interpretation methods yield the most accurate Vs or G0 values?
1
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:12==
• Can current interpretation methods be improved to reduce subjectivity?
• Is it possible to establish correlations between the soil parameters and Vs or G0?
This thesis report consists of four main chapters. The first chapter, titled State of the art, provides an
overview of current research on BE, RC, triaxial, and DSS tests. The chapter describes the current
interpretation methods used in BE testing, as well as the issues and limitations of these methods. In
addition, the purpose and relevance of RC testing is described in this thesis. Furthermore, it reviews
previous research on triaxial tests and issues with obtaining the shear degradation curves from
these. Finally, an improved saturation method of the DSS tests is described to obtain results which
are comparable to those of the corresponding triaxial tests. The second chapter, Methodology, goes
through the experimental test setups, the laboratory program, and the methods used to obtain the
results in this thesis. The third chapter, Results, presents the key results derived from the data
processing described in Methodology. Lastly, the Analysis and discussion chapter combines the
insights of each of the previous chapters, for analysis and discussion of the results obtained. The
results of the thesis, as well as the findings of other authors, will be compared and discussed. For
the uncertainties which are still present, suggestions for future work are described.
2
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:13==
2
State of the art
2.1
Shear modulus degradation curves
Shear modulus degradation curves are a deformation characteristic of soils, which presents the
response to the degradation of the shear modulus G as the strain increases. In general, soil behavior
is plastic and non-linear, however, at very small shear strains (γ ≤10−3 %) the soil behavior can
be assumed to be linear elastic (Soból et al., 2020). In the linear elastic region of the soil, the small
strain shear modulus G0 can be determined. To obtain shear degradation curves, different tests are
conducted on the same type of soil, under the same density and loading conditions. In Figure 2.1
a concept illustration of a shear degradation curve can be observed, with an indication of the tests
used to cover a specific strain range.
Figure 2.1: Illustration of a shear degradation curve. Relevant tests used to obtain the shear modu-
lus in a specific strain range are shown under the x-axis. The figure is adapted from Atkinson and
Sallfors (1991).
As can be seen, the BE test covers the very small to small strain range (γ ∈[10−6 %10−3 %]), and
the RC test covers the very small strain and part of the intermediate strain ranges (γ ≤10−2 %).
Therefore, both of these tests can be used to determine G0. From the intermediate to large strain
range (γ ≥10−3 %), triaxial tests with local strain measurements can be used to determine the
shear degradation curve in the non-linear plastic region. Lastly, conventional tests can be used in
the larger strain range (γ ≥10−1 %). In the case of this thesis, the DSS test was used to cover the
larger strain range.
3
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:14==
2.2
Soil testing with bender element implementation
2.2.1
Bender element function
A BE is a piezoelectric transducer, which converts electrical energy into mechanical energy, and
vice versa (Arulnathan et al., 1998). BEs are therefore able to deform when an electric signal is
applied but also deform and convert the deformation into an electrical signal. In soil testing, this
is taken advantage of to obtain the shear wave velocity Vs of a test specimen during testing. A set
of BEs, consisting of one BE acting as a transmitter and another BE acting as a receiver, can be
installed in a specimen on the opposite sides of a test specimen, as illustrated in Figure 2.2.
Figure 2.2: Illustration showing how the BEs are installed in the soil specimen. When an electrical
signal is introduced in the transmitter BE, the deformation creates waves in the soil. The waves
propagates through the soil, displacing the receiver BE where the displacement is converted into
electrical energy. The figure is adapted from VJ Tech Limited (2024).
An electrical signal can be applied to the transmitter BE by the use of a function generator. The
function generator will generate a waveform, for example a sinusoidal waveform at a given input
frequency, which when applied to the BE creates a swinging motion in the BE. The swinging
motion will generate waves in the soil, which travel through the soil specimen and are absorbed by
the receiver BE. The receiver BE will be displaced by these waves, converting the displacement
into an electrical signal that can be logged by a digital oscilloscope. The received signal can
then be interpreted by the operator using visualization software. The waves that are generated
by the displacement of the transmitter BE, consist of shear waves (S-waves) and two compression
waves (P-waves) (Lee & Santamarina, 2005), as shown in Figure 2.2. A common issue in BE
interpretation in soils is that the P-waves travel faster than the S-waves, thus arriving at the receiver
BE before the S-wave (Rio, 2006). Therefore, the arrival of the P-wave may be misinterpreted as
the arrival of the S-wave. Thus, Vs and G0 is overestimated.
4
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:15==
2.2.2
Usage
BEs can be installed in various test setups, including triaxial, cyclic triaxial, oedometer, and more.
BEs are usually installed on the base pedestal and top cap of the specimen, but can also be installed
horizontally by puncturing the membrane of the specimen (Chaney et al., 2001). This approach
is used to study the soil anisotropy, especially in clay specimens. The user can conduct BE mea-
surements at any stage of the test, however, most commonly the measurements are conducted after
reaching the end of primary consolidation (EOP) at the consolidation stage. When conducting BE
measurements, the frequency of the waveform should be close to the resonance frequency of the
system (Lee & Santamarina, 2005). This is due to the amplitude of the received signal reaching
a maximum, allowing for improved visualization and interpretation of the signal. The resonant
frequency can be determined by increasing or decreasing the input signal frequency while search-
ing for the maximum amplitude in the received signal. The resonance frequency depends on the
overall stiffness of the system (Lee & Santamarina, 2005).
2.2.3
Signal types
Lee and Santamarina (2005) showed that the configuration of the input wave influences the ampli-
tude of the output signal. The configuration refers to the waveform of the electric signal generated
by the function generator. The majority of the applied excitations can be grouped into configura-
tions used in prior BE research by several authors:
• Step excitation (Dyvik & Madshus, 1985) and (Fam & Santamarina, 1995).
• Sine wave (Viggiani & Atkinson, 1995) and (Brignoli et al., 1996).
• Harmonic excitation (Greening & Nash, 2004).
• Stochastic excitation (Roesler, 1979) and (Santos et al., 2007).
In this thesis, burst signals of different waveforms, and sinusoidal sweep signals were applied.
Burst signal
Burst signals are characterized by their finite duration, which yields clear areas for interpretation
of the S-wave arrival in the received data. Burst signals are constructed by the function generator
by creating a full period of a signal, followed by silence. The advantage of burst signals is the
possibility of interpretation both in the time domain and in the frequency domain of the received
signal (Rio, 2006). Typical burst signals can be seen from Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Illustration of the signal waveforms interpreted in this thesis. (a) Sinusoidal. (b)
Ramp. (c) Square. The signals are illustrated as burst signals. The illustrations are adapted from
Rio (2006).
5
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:16==
Sweep signal
Sweep signals consist of non-harmonic sinusoidal continuous signals. Here, the frequency varies
with time. A sweep signal can be described using Equation 2.1 (Rio, 2006).
y(t) = A sin(ω(t)t + θ)
,
ω(t) = ω(0) + ∂ω
∂t t
(2.1)
Where A is the amplitude of the signal, ω(t) is the excitation frequency as a function of time t, and
θ is the phase step. An example of this function can be seen in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Example of a sinusoidal sweep, adapted from Rio (2006).
The signal starts at a lower frequency, but as the time increases on the x-axis, the frequency in-
creases, yielding shorter periods for each cycle. The advantage of the sweep signal is that a wider
frequency content is obtained.
2.2.4
Signal waveforms
In this thesis, three waveforms are studied: sinusoidal, ramp, and square. Conceptual illustrations
of each waveform can be seen in Figure 2.3. BEs, because of their finite mass and stiffness, cannot
perfectly replicate the input signals. As noted by Rio (2006), BEs behave as Newtonian mechanical
systems, thus resulting in non-instantaneous responses. Consequently, when the transmitter BE is
excited, the output signals exhibit a sinusoidal waveform. The output signal gradually increases in
amplitude until reaching a peak, followed by a damping phase where the amplitude decreases to
approximately zero. This behavior highlights a discrepancy between the input signal and the actual
response of the transmitter BE, which consequently affects the characteristics of the received signal.
2.3
Interpretation of bender element data
2.3.1
Travel distance
Problems on how to determine the effective travel distance Leff have been discussed by Rio
(2006). In previous tests, Leff was assessed as the minimum distance between the two BEs, also
known as the tip-to-tip distance Ltt. However, other studies concluded that Leff measured be-
tween about 60 % of the embedment height of the BEs L60, yielded better results than Ltt (Rio,
2006). This coincides with the estimation of the centers of dynamic pressure exerted by the trans-
mitter BE on the soil specimen. Both of these distances are found using the specimen height H,
as well as the BE height Hb, see Figure 2.5.
6
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:17==
Ltt
L60
Soil 
specimen
Bender 
element
H
Hb
Figure 2.5: Illustration of effective distances in a typical soil specimen with BEs.
However, in general, the travel distance is seen to be of insignificant importance compared to the
arrival time t. This is especially the case for specimens that are significantly taller than Hb, thus
decreasing the dependency on Hb.
2.3.2
Time domain interpretation
The time domain interpretation methods assume that the Vs can be determined by the arrival time
of the S-waves, at the receiver BE. The most common interpretation methods, which are described
in the ASTM standard (ASTM International, 2019), are ”start to start” and ”peak to peak”. Other
interpretation methods include ”first sharp”, ”first bump”, ”zero crossing”, and ”cross-correlation”
as described by Moldovan et al. (2024). Furthermore, Moldovan et al. (2024) describes the second
arrival of the output wave. In this case, the S-wave reflects from the top of the specimen, back to the
transmitter BE and then reflects to the receiver BE for a second arrival. This removes uncertainties
in both the travel time and the travel distance. This is because the travel time is equal to the
time between the first and second arrival, while the travel distance will be twice the height of the
specimen. However, this method has the disadvantage that the S-waves attenuate as they travel
through the specimen, and therefore the signal amplitude is decreased. Thus, a clear visual arrival
time may not be available. An illustration of the input signal and the first and second arrivals of
the signal can be seen in Figure 2.6.
7
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:18==
Figure 2.6: Illustration showing a typical plot of an input signal (dashed blue line) and an output
signal (solid red line), with markings of different interpretation methods. The figure is adapted
from Moldovan et al. (2024)
First arrival
First arrival (FA) interpretation is based on the time between the moment the input signal is sent
(point A) and a clear change of amplitude is observed in the received signal. The FA can be
interpreted in different ways. The first sharp (point C), the first bump (point D), and the zero
crossing (point E) are all viable options to determine Vs. The second arrival can be determined by
the time between the first arrival and the corresponding point C’, D’, or E’. However, as described
by Moldovan et al. (2024), it is difficult to determine which of these points is the actual first arrival
of the S-wave. Furthermore, the presence of P-waves can pollute the readings, thereby concealing
the exact time of arrival of the first S-wave. This phenomenon is also known as the near-field
effects.
Peak to peak
Peak to peak (PTP) interpretation is the time between the first peak in the input signal (point B),
to the first peak in the received signal (point F). PTP can also be interpreted using second arrival,
by the time between point F and point F’.
Cross-correlation
The cross-correlation (CC) method measures the degree of correlation of two signals x(T) and
y(T). Using the cross-correlation between two signals, a maximum correlation is found at a given
time shift τ. This time shift corresponds to the travel time of the S-wave. However, as described by
Moldovan et al. (2024) the arrival of the S-wave may not correspond to the largest oscillation in the
received signal, and therefore the absolute maximum of the cross-correlation may not correspond to
the arrival of the S-wave. However, the advantage of the CC method is that it is objective, whereas
the FA and PTP interpretation methods have some degree of subjectivity attached to them. The
CC can be written as Equation 2.2 (Viggiani & Atkinson, 1995).
CCxy(t) =
lim
Tr→∞
1
Tr
∫
Tr
x(T)y(T + τ)dT
(2.2)
Where Tr is the recorded time.
2.3.3
Frequency domain interpretation
To avoid uncertainties from the time domain interpretation methods, another way of interpreting
BE data is through the frequency domain interpretation methods. As described by Moldovan et al.
(2024), three methods are usually applied. These include the discrete π-point method, the cross-
spectrum method, and the continuous method. As most of the BE data used in this thesis apply
burst input signals, only the cross-spectrum method is of relevance.
8
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:19==
Cross-spectrum method
In the cross-spectrum method, fast Fourier transformation (FFT) is applied to the input and received
signal, to obtain the phase angle between the signals. Then, the travel time can be calculated by
the slope of the frequency-unwrapped phase plot ϕ(f), as shown in Equation 2.3 (Moldovan et al.,
2024).
t = 1
2π
dϕ(f)
d(f)
(2.3)
The issue of frequency domain interpretation is that studies consistently report a lower Vs, com-
pared to the time domain methods (Ogino et al., 2015). Despite research efforts over the past
30 years, the underlying causes remain undocumented, and no definitive conclusion has been ob-
tained.
2.3.4
GeoHyTE interpretation
To remove subjectivity in the interpretation of BE data, the software ”GeoHyTE” was developed
(Moldovan et al., 2024). The software is Matlab based and uses FE modeling for calculation of
the receiver BE motion. The FE model utilizes hybrid-Trefftz finite elements, chosen because of
their insensitivity to large frequencies and small wavelengths. Hybrid-Trefftz finite elements have
shown significant advantages in modeling transient wave propagation problems, which BE exper-
iments are characterized by. Their validation was presented by Moldovan et al. (2016), and their
application to the automatic calculation of G0 was described by Moldovan and Gomes Correia
(2017). Finally, they were integrated into GeoHyTE (Moldovan et al., 2024). Unlike other dis-
placement elements, hybrid-Trefftz elements employ physically meaningful approximation bases
for displacement fields, utilizing frequency dependent functions. This allows the approximation
functions to differentiate between the various types of waves which are developed by the dynamic
response of the soil specimen. An example for their usage is for two P-waves and an S-wave in sat-
urated porous soil (de Freitas et al., 2011), as is the case in this thesis. Hybrid-Trefftz elements are
30 times less sensitive to wavelength than other types of elements, which allows utilization of very
coarse meshes. Additionally, the integration of wavelet based time integration techniques enables
the usage of large time steps, which significantly improves the efficiency of the calculations.
The software creates a received signal similar to that of laboratory tests, using calculations based
on the boundary conditions and the physics of wave propagation in the soil. The advantage of
GeoHyTE is that minimal user interpretation is required to construct the model. Most input pa-
rameters are soil specific and obtained from classification and advanced laboratory tests. These
input parameters are used for generating a visual representation of the wave propagation, as well as
a simulated signal from the receiver BE. In addition to the signal created by the FE calculations, the
displacement and stress fields can be plotted using a visualization software. Using visualization
software, one can see the FE displacement fields at increasing time increments, which allows for
visual interpretation of the P- and S-wave arrivals.
2.4
Effect of soil parameters and dimensions on bender element
measurements
Different soil parameters affect the propagation of waves. This includes the degree of saturation,
dry and bulk density, permeability, and specimen size. In this section, these parameters are de-
scribed.
2.4.1
Saturation
Previous research has shown that the dynamic properties of sand in small strains are heavily influ-
enced by the degree of saturation Sr. Khan et al. (2018) found that G and Young’s modulus E in
dry sand, was significantly higher, than in 50 % saturated and fully saturated sand. Furthermore, it
9
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:20==
was shown that the decrease in G and E was non-linear, and the difference between 75 % saturation
and full saturation was minimal. The same observations were seen by Gu et al. (2021), however,
in this study a wider range of Skempton B values were showcased. It was found that after reaching
around B = 0.355, Vs stopped decreasing significantly. Therefore, even at low Sr values, Vs was
observed to decrease. Based on these observations, this decrease was proposed to be caused by an
increase in the mass density, as well as a decrease in G due to loss of capillary forces. However,
as described in subsection 2.4.2, the increase in density is expected to increase Vs. Therefore, the
explanation of the loss of capillary forces seems more viable.
2.4.2
Density
The Vs has previously been shown to increase with density, as shown by Anbazhagan et al. (2016).
In this study, a correlation was found between bulk density ρ and Vs, as well as dry density ρd and
Vs. The correlation between ρd and Vs was found to yield higher values of Vs compared to the
correlation between ρ and Vs. This further strengthens the claim that soil saturation decreases Vs,
as described in the previous section. The increase in Vs by the increase in ρd is caused by larger
compaction of the grains in the soil. This causes the material to become stiffer, thereby allowing
for faster wave propagation of the S-waves. This also indicates an increase in Vs with a decrease
in the void ratio e, as also stated by Abuawad et al. (2024).
2.4.3
Stress state
Abuawad et al. (2024) showed that Vs is highly dependent on the stress state of the soil. It was
shown that an increase in effective confining stress increased Vs. The increase was highly non-
linear, producing lower increases of Vs as the confining stress increased. Lee and Santamarina
(2005) showed a ratio between the P- and S-wave velocities as Vp/Vs, which is stress dependent
for saturated soils. The ratio decreased as the effective stress increased. Therefore, it is expected
that the P-wave arrives significantly earlier than the S-waves, under saturated low-stress conditions.
2.4.4
Permeability
In a previous study conducting BE and permeability tests in kaolin clay, a correlation between
water content w and permeability, as well as w and Vs was found (Mazzieri et al., 2001). As w
increased, the permeability κ was found to decrease. In the same tests, when the w increased,
Vs was found to decrease. Therefore, an indirect correlation could be found, suggesting that an
increase in κ would yield an increase in Vs.
2.4.5
Specimen size
Previous numerical studies conducted by Arroyo et al. (2006) have shown that a specimen size
effect was present when conducting BE tests. Finite-difference models were created for specimens
of different dimensions. In this study, it was concluded that errors in Vs estimates using the finite-
difference method were increased by slender geometries, high wavelengths, reflecting boundaries,
and low material attenuation. In the same study, the travel distance was researched. It was found
that when lateral reflections were numerically excluded, Vs estimates converged towards the true Vs
as the travel distance was increased. Therefore, to obtain proper BE measurements, larger specimen
sizes are preferred. At travel distances greater than 3λ, where λ is the apparent wavelength, it was
found that near-field effects could be avoided. Therefore, when utilizing BE in tests with a small
travel distance, the near-field effects become more difficult to trace and avoid. In another study by
Gao et al. (2022), the wave attenuation in clay specimens was studied experimentally. It was found
that Vs on average was underestimated by 10 % using FA interpretation methods on specimens
with heights greater than 80 mm. Wave attenuation is also expected for sand specimens, and thus
deviations caused by wave attenuation are expected during testing.
Another study by Almukashfi et al. (2024) proposed a method to reduce near-field effects using
a modified Rowe cell with BEs. Here, a transmitter BE and receiver BEs were installed in the
bottom of the Rowe cell, with the possibility of installing the receiver BEs at different locations
10
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:21==
and at different distances to the transmitter BE. This configuration made it possible to conduct echo
tests, which allowed to capture the arrival time of the horizontal P-waves, as well as the vertical
S-waves. In Figure 2.7 the modified Rowe cell is illustrated.
Figure 2.7: Illustration of the modified Rowe cell BE setup. The figure is adapted from Almukashfi
et al. (2024).
The setup in the figure contains two receiver BEs, with different distances. The S-waves are cap-
tured after reflecting from the exchangeable reflecting boundary, followed by arrival at the receiver
BE. The P-waves, however, travel in the horizontal plane and arrive at the receiver BEs. The ad-
vantage of this setup is an accurate determination of the P-wave and S-wave arrivals, which greatly
reduces the near-field effects. Furthermore, the height of the specimen and the top plate can be
changed. This allows for the definition of the height effects of the specimen as well as the reflec-
tion of the boundaries on different materials. Finally, wave attenuation can be assessed, due to
the adjustable distancing of the receiver BEs. Almukashfi et al. (2024) found that an increase in
distance between receivers would result in a decrease in Vs.
2.5
Resonant column
The RC test is used to obtain the G and damping properties of soil in small strain ranges (ASTM
International, 2021). The test utilizes torsional and/or flexural excitation, in which the resulting soil
behavior is used to solve the equation of wave propagation using the theory of elasticity (Cascante
et al., 1998). BE tests were compared to RC tests in previous studies, as both test methods are used
to obtain G0 (Camacho-Tauta et al., 2013). Therefore, the RC test was used as a comparison tool
for the BE tests, where it was assumed that RC yielded the actual Vs and G0 of the soil specimen.
Two types of RC setup are described, namely the Stokoe type described by Cascante et al. (1998),
and the Hardin type described by Li et al. (2018). The Stokoe-type setup is a fixed-free setup that
is used for isotropic loading conditions. The Hardin-type setup is fixed-fixed, with the ability of
applying an axial stress using an actuator, and thereby obtain anisotropic conditions.
11
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:22==
2.6
Triaxial testing
The triaxial test is used to determine the shear strength parameters and the stiffness moduli of soils
(International Organization for Standardization, 2018a). An advantage of the triaxial test is its
ability to define the stress state of a soil specimen in two principle axes. These are the axial stress
and the confining stress. This allows for highly versatile testing as it allows the application of
different stress states as well as the use of additional measuring equipment, such as BEs and local
strain LVDTs.
2.6.1
Triaxial testing with local strain measurements
Measurement of local strain in triaxial testing is the process of attaching linear variable differential
transformers (LVDTs) directly to the test specimen. The usual procedure for strain measurements
involves attaching an LVDT to the piston rod outside of the triaxial test cell, which measures the
axial deformation caused by the piston rod. The advantage of the local LVDTs is precise measure-
ments of the deformation directly on the soil specimen. Thus, the definition of G is available in
lower strain ranges. The external LVDT, in principle, only measures the displacement of the piston
rod, thus assuming the specimen deformation is equal to this displacement, which is not always
true.
To obtain shear degradation curves in the intermediate strain range, previous studies have used
the implementation of local LVDTs on triaxial specimens (Carotenuto et al., 2023). The research
found that repeatable and reliable shear degradation curves for cyclic consolidated anisotropically
drained triaxial tests (CADcy), could be determined. However, for static parallel CAD tests, a
significant difference was found in the shear modulus calculated at small strains (γ ≤0.002 %). It
was not possible to explain why the static CAD tests deviated.
2.6.2
Dilatancy
Dilatancy is described by the change in volume as a response to shear deformation in soils. The
dilatancy is measured as an angle, described as the angle of dilatancy ψ. A soil of high relative
density Dr, therefore, a low initial void ratio e0, will show an initial slight decrease in volume,
followed by an increase in volume. The specimen then reaches a constant volume with continued
shearing, as described by Keaton (2018). This state of the soil is known as the critical state (CS)
and is described in soil tests as the critical state line (CSL). Furthermore, a low Dr will contract
and thereby decrease in volume until a constant volume is reached. Thereby, no CSL is observed
as described by Bolton (1986). Based on the theory described by Keaton (2018), it was suggested
that due to the change in e, a correlation between the maximum angle of dilatancy ψmax, and the
Vs of the soil may be present.
2.6.3
Critical state parameter
The behavior of sand is density and stress dependent. Therefore, the critical state parameter Ψ
is used as a sand characterization parameter under the given stress and density conditions (Phan
et al., 2016a). The Ψ can be defined as Equation 3.52 by the e0 subtracted by the void ratio at the
critical state eCSL. It was suggested that due to the CSL being repeatable (Santamarina & Cho,
2001), and Vs being correlated to the pore structure of the specimen, a correlation between the Ψ
and Vs may be obtained.
2.7
Direct simple shear testing
The DSS test is used to obtain the monotonic shear characteristics of soil. The key parameters
obtained in DSS testing are the shear stress τ and γ, which can be used to obtain the G during
testing (ASTM International, 2017).
2.7.1
Influence of saturation
Studies conducted have shown that CO2 flushing silt specimens before saturation yielded CSL
which aligns much better with parallel triaxial tests (Reid et al., 2024). The CO2 flushing ensures
12
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:23==
that air is pushed out of the specimen, prior to saturation with de-aired tap water, to increase the
Sr of the test specimen.
13
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:24==

Methodology
3.1
Material
The sand used in this thesis was from a previous project by Lunne et al. (2019), where a new method
was developed to determine the minimum and maximum density of sands. The sand was from the
Borkum Riffgrund 02 wind farm outside the coast of Germany and will be referred to as ”BKR02”
throughout this thesis. The sand was subsampled at depths of 3.0 to 48.0 m below the seafloor,
and formed into larger site-specific batches. Therefore, the test specifications were designed to
acquire effective stresses within these ranges. The BKR02 sand was a fine to medium, poorly
graded, subrounded to rounded silica sand with approximately 86 % quartz and a fines content of
about 14 %. The characteristics of the BKR02 sand can be seen in Table 3.1, while the particle

Figure 3.2: Photo of the closed-bin riffle splitter used for homogenization of the sand. a) Collection

The sand was subsampled in smaller sizes, which corresponded to the volume of one collection
bin. The first subsample was poured into the riffle splitter, dividing the subsample into two halves.
Then, one of the bins was replaced by an empty bin, and the bin was emptied into the riffle splitter.
This procedure was repeated at least four times for each subsample, before collecting all subsam-
ples in a large container. The entire process was repeated to ensure proper homogenization.
3.2
Laboratory equipment
3.2.1
Bender element setup
The BEs were constructed in-house at Geo, using piezoceramics covered in polyurethane. The
transmitter BE was installed in the bottom pedestal and the receiver BE was installed in the top
head of the triaxial setup, as can be seen in Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Photo of BEs installed in the top head and bottom pedestal.
The BE in the bottom pedestal functioned as the transmitter BE, while the BE in the top head
was used as the receiver. The transmitter was connected to the function generator and the receiver
was connected to the oscilloscope. The function generator and oscilloscope were connected to a
laptop with the PicoScope software installed, which was used for the interpretation of the signals
and data acquisition. The setup can be seen from Figure 3.4. It was important when conducting
BE tests that the BEs were aligned parallel to each other to avoid drift. Drift is a low-frequency
16
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:27==
disturbance that can be caused by the BEs not being aligned, electrical issues, or mechanical issues
(Fernández-Lavín et al., 2023).
Figure 3.4: Photo of the BE setup. 1) Function Generator. 2) Oscilloscope. 3) Laptop used for
data acquisition.
Function generator
The function generator used to generate an electrical signal, which was applied to the transmitter
BE, was from Agilent (Agilent, 2024). The function generator was capable of producing different
signal types, with the option to adjust the frequency, amplitude, and delay of the signal.
Oscilloscope
The oscilloscope used to acquire the receiver BE signal was from Picotech (Picotech, 2024). The
oscilloscope was connected to the laptop, which had the Picotech software PicoScope installed.
3.2.2
Resonant column setup
The RC setup used in this thesis was a Stokoe-type setup from GDS Instruments (GDS Instruments,
2024). Photos of the test setup can be seen in Figures 3.5 and 3.6.
17
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:28==
Figure 3.5: Photo of the RC setup, with a specimen installed. 1) RC stiffening top plate. 2) LVDT
housing. 3) Top head. 4) Coil housing with two coils. 5) Top head back pressure tubes. 6) RC
frame. 7) Magnet between two coils. 8) Counterweight for accelerometer. 9) Drive plate. 10)
Accelerometer. 11) Water tub. 12) Magnet bolt used for alignment of driving plate.
The setup consisted of a bottom pedestal, where the specimen was installed. The test specimens
were compacted in a latex membrane using the Ladd undercompaction method (Ladd, 1978) de-
scribed in subsection 3.5.1. The latex membrane was tested for leakages by applying air pressure
and submerging it in water, checking for air bubbles escaping from the membrane. After the spec-
imen was compacted, the top head was placed on top of the specimen, secured by two o-rings at
the top and bottom of the membrane. Two screws were inserted into the top head, which sealed the
system. A vacuum of approximately 15 kPa was applied to the specimen and the split mold was
removed. To assess whether the membrane was punctured during reconstitution, all back pressure
valves were closed, and it was observed if the membrane would contain the vacuum. If no leakage
was observed, the RC frame could be installed. Then, the screws that sealed the top head were
removed and two tubes were connected to the top head. One for the back pressure and one for the
expelling of the pore fluid. After this, the driving device could be installed.
Around the specimen, a small water tub was placed, which was used to apply radial stress to the
specimen during testing. The water tub was kept waterproof at the bottom, by the use of an o-ring
between the bottom pedestal and the tub. The tub was open at the top, and therefore, a thin layer
of silicone oil was applied, to prevent the water from evaporating during the tests. The tub was
filled to just above the first o-ring, ensuring an evenly distributed stress on the specimen. For the
installation of the driving device on top of the specimen, a metal frame was placed around the water
tub. The frame was secured with screws, which were fixed in the bottom pedestal. A driving device
consisting of four coil housings and a driving plate was carefully placed on top of the steel frame,
with the driving plate resting on top of the specimen top head. The coil housings of the driving
device consisted of two coils in each and a magnet placed inside the coils, which was connected
to the driving plate. The driving plate was attached to the top head using four screws. The driving
device could be adjusted using four screws that were resting on the metal frame, thus raising or
18
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:29==
lowering the driving device. The driving device was adjusted such that the screws on the magnets
aligned with the hole in each magnet housing, as can be seen in Figure 3.5(12). Furthermore, the
driving plate was adjusted so that the magnets were not in contact with the coils, thereby ensuring
that the driving plate rests on the specimen top head. Four screws were attached to the metal frame
and tightened, thereby keeping the driving device fixed in place. Every coil housing was connected
to the function generator of the setup, through cables to the bottom pedestal. A cable was attached
to the accelerometer to measure the response of the system when excitation was applied. A top
plate was installed on top of the driving device, using eight screws. The top plate acted as a stiffener
for the driving device, as well as an LVDT housing unit. Lastly, an LVDT was placed on the top
plate, with the LVDT needle resting on top of the driving plate. The cell was then installed, as can
be seen in Figure 3.6.
2
3
4
1
5
Figure 3.6: Photo of the RC setup with the cell and LVDT installed. 1) Cell. 2) Valve connecting
the cell to the air pressure regulator. 3) Valve used to connect the cell to the surrounding air. 4)
LVDT. 5) Tightening screws.
The cell of the RC setup was fixed to the bottom pedestal using six large screws. In between the
cell and the pedestal, o-rings were placed to ensure the setup was airtight. When cell pressure was
applied, the valve to the surrounding air was closed, while the valve to the air regulator was opened
to the cell. Before testing, a pressure test was performed in which a dummy specimen was installed
and an effective stress of 300 kPa was applied. The back pressure volume was then observed to
be constant in the short term while changing in the long term due to temperature change. Thus, no
leakage was observed between the cell and the test specimen.

Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:30==
Back pressure pump
The back pressure pump was from GDS Instruments and was used to apply back pressure inside
the test specimen, during testing.
Pore pressure transducer
The pore pressure transducer used to measure the stress in the specimen was from Druck (Baker
Hughes, 2025), and had a capacity of 60 MPa.
LVDT
The LVDT was from RDPE (RDP Electronics Ltd., 2024). The LVDT had a range of ±10 mm in
the negative/positive range, for a total range of 20 mm.
Driving system
The RC driving system was from GDS Instruments, and served as the excitation unit of the system.
The system consisted of coils and magnets, which delivered the excitation, and an accelerometer,
which measured the response of the soil.
Function generator
The function generator was from GDS Instruments and was used to generate a sinusoidal electrical
signal at a given amplitude and frequency, which was applied to the coils. This generated an
electromagnetic field, which displaced the magnets inside the coils.
Air pressure regulator
The air pressure regulator from GDS Instruments was used to regulate the air pressure, which was
applied inside the cell. The regulator was connected to a compressor, which delivered a 1 MPa air
pressure to the regulator. The regulator adjusted the amount of air that was allowed into the cell to
control the cell pressure.
Data logger
The data logger consisted of the GDS Instruments data logger. All measuring equipment was
connected to this unit, which recorded the output signal in volts on the computer. The software
installed on the computer could then convert the voltage into a given unit using a calibration factor
for the given transducer.
3.2.3
Triaxial setup
The triaxial setup used to conduct the CID tests with BEs and local strain LVDTs, can be seen in
Figure 3.7. A scale could be connected to the triaxial setup, to conduct permeability tests, as seen
in Figure 3.8.
20
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:31==
Figure 3.7: Photo of the triaxial setup, with a specimen installed. 1) External Force transducer. 2)
External LVDT. 3) Piston rod. 4) 1700 kPa pressure cell. 5) Tie rods. 6) Top head with back pres-

Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:32==
Figure 3.8: Photo of the triaxial setup, converted to the permeability setup. 1) Valve connecting
the expelled fluid to the water bottle on the scale. 2) Water bottle storing the water expelled from
the specimen. A thin layer of silicone oil was spread out on top of the water, to avoid evaporation
of the water. 3) Scale measuring the mass of the water bottle.
The setup consisted of a pressure cell with a piston rod connected to a force actuator. The actuator
adjusted the axial displacement applied to the test specimen. The applied force was measured
through a force transducer connected to the force actuator. Two pumps were connected to the
bottom pedestal of the cell, where one acted as the cell pressure and the other as the back pressure
inside the specimen. Two external pressure transducers measured the pore pressure in the specimen
and the cell pressure, respectively. The deformation in the specimen was measured with an external
LVDT and two internal local LVDTs. The LVDT and BE cables were routed through a steel ring
frame positioned between the cell and the bottom pedestal. The ring frame had waterproof openings
for the cables, which ensured that there was no leakage when the cell pressure increased. The
test specimen was installed inside the membrane using the Ladd undercompaction method (Ladd,
1978). In the bottom pedestal, a BE was installed. Therefore, the first soil layer was carefully
compacted, to avoid damaging the BE. Following compaction, the top head was installed on the
specimen, ensuring alignment of the BEs, using guidelines drawn onto the bottom pedestal. The
membrane was then secured using two o-rings at the bottom and at the top of the membrane. Before
continuation, the BE alignment was assessed by applying a burst signal and checking the received
signal. If a clear signal was obtained, the test could continue. If drift was observed, the top head
was re-aligned, until a proper signal was observed. Afterward, local LVDTs were installed on
both sides of the specimen. Finally, the pressure cell was installed, secured by five tie rods, and
sealed at the bottom by an o-ring. Before testing, the test setup was pressure tested using a dummy
specimen. Cell pressure was increased and an effective stress of 300 kPa was applied to the dummy
specimen. The back volume was observed, where in the short term it was constant, while in the
long term the volume changed as a result of temperature change.
Pressure cell
The pressure cell was from ELE International (ELE International, 2024), capable of resisting a
pressure of up to 1700 kPa.
22
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:33==
Force Transducer
A 25 kN force transducer from GDS Instruments was used for half of the triaxial tests. The force
transducer measured the axial force applied to the specimen, which was used to calculate the axial
stress. For the remaining tests, an internal 4 kN force transducer from GDS was used, to obtain
higher precision in force measurements.
Force actuator
A stepper motor force actuator from GDS Instruments was used for the triaxial tests. The force
actuator applied the axial displacement to the specimen and was used for shearing and to adjust
the axial stress applied to the specimen.
Pressure pumps
Two pressure pumps from GDS Instruments were used for the triaxial tests. One of the pumps was
the cell pressure pump, which applied pressure inside the cell and acted on the test specimen as
radial stresses (σ2 = σ3). The second pump was the back pressure pump, which applied pressure
inside the specimen. With the use of the two pumps, the effective stress between the specimen and
cell pressure could be controlled.
Pressure transducers
A 2.5 MPa pressure transducer from Keller (Keller, 2024) was used for each pump, to ensure correct
appliance of pressure. As the pumps were not leveled with the test specimen, the height difference
caused a deviation in pressure from the pumps to the cell/specimen. Therefore, a pore pressure
transducer and a cell pressure transducer were connected to the bottom pedestal to measure actual
pressures.
Deformation gauges
An external TR-050 LVDT from Novotechnik was used to measure the axial displacement of the
test specimen. The LVDT could accurately measure displacements of up to 50 mm.
For higher precision, two submersible internal local LVDTs from RDPE were placed 180˚ opposite
of each other, directly on the specimen. The LVDTs had a range of ±5 mm in the negative/positive
measuring area, for a total range of 10 mm.
Scale
The scale used for the permeability tests was from Mettler Toledo (Mettler Toledo, 2025). The
scale had three sliding doors, which blocked air from interfering with the scale. The scale could
accurately show the weight of an object down to the 3rd decimal point.
Data logger
The data logger used to collect measurements was an Agilent 43970A data acquisition unit. All
measuring apparatuses were connected to this unit, which recorded the output signal in volts on
the computer. The software installed on the computer could then convert the voltage into a given
unit using a calibration factor for the given transducer.
3.2.4
Direct simple shear setup
The DSS setup used in this thesis can be seen from Figure 3.9.
23
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:34==
Figure 3.9: Photo of the DSS setup. 1) ShearTrac-II. 2) Water container. 3) Horizontal force
transducer. 4) Vertical actuator rod. 5) Vertical piston rod confinement arm. 6) Horizontal LVDT.
7) Computer with Geocomp software installed for data acquisition. 8) Specimen submerged in
water bath. 9) Vertical force transducer. 10) Vertical LVDT.
The main component of the DSS setup was the ShearTrac-II from Geocomp (Geocomp, 2024),
which applied the displacements on the specimen. The ShearTrac-II could apply displacements
in the vertical direction in case of consolidation, as well as the horizontal direction for shearing
purposes. To install the specimen, the specimen was compacted using the Ladd undercompaction
method (Ladd, 1978), inside a membrane surrounded by friction plates on a bottom pedestal. After
compaction, a top head was installed on top of the specimen, which had an opening for the vertical
displacement rod. In the top head and bottom pedestal, protruding pins were used to limit slippage.
The membrane was kept waterproof by placing two o-rings at the bottom, as well as one on the top
head. The specimen was placed inside the water container, without being initially filled with water,
while pushing the bottom pedestal against the side of the water container. The vertical piston rod
confinement arm was lowered, such that the piston rod was vertically aligned. The piston rod was
carefully lowered to assess if it could slide into the top head. If friction was observed, the water
container was carefully adjusted horizontally until alignment was reached. The piston rod was then
raised so that it was floating above the top head, followed by zeroing the vertical and horizontal
force. The actuator was then driven to it’s top position, and then lowered to a point, which allowed
for sufficient travel distance during the consolidation and shear phase. The piston rod was then
lowered again until it had full contact with the top head. The vertical LVDT was installed on top
of the actuator beam. A seating pressure was applied using the Geocomp control software. This
was followed by tightening the screws in the water container to fix the bottom pedestal, the top
head of the specimen to fix the piston rod, and the vertical piston rod confinement arm.
LVDT
The LVDTs were from Novotechnik and were used to measure the deformation in the horizontal
and vertical directions. The LVDTs had a measuring range of 25 mm.
Force transducer
The force transducers were from Interface (Interface Inc., 2025), and consisted of a horizontal and
vertical force transducer. The capacity of each transducer was 8.9 kN.
24
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:35==
Datalogger
The ShearTrac-II utilized an internal datalogger, which was connected to the computer, for storage
of the transducer voltages. The computer software then converted the voltages into specific units
using a calibration factor.
3.3
Software
Throughout this thesis, various software were used for data processing, control of test setups, acqui-
sition of data, calibration of equipment, and figure creation. For data processing purposes Python,
Microsoft Excel, Maple and ParaView were used. Each of these had a specific purpose that made
them preferred for each task. Furthermore, the Matlab based software GeoHyTE was used for
interpretation of the BE measurements, as described earlier. The software used for the control of
test setups and data acquisition consisted of Picotech PicoScope for the BE measurements, GDS
Instruments GDSLab and GDS RCA for the RC tests, Geo’s in-house software Lablog for the triax-
ial tests, and Geocomp Shear DSS for the DSS tests. For calibration of equipment, Geo’s in-house
calibration software Geocalib was used.
3.3.1
Python
Python is a general-purpose open-source programming language. Python was used for the data pro-
cessing of the test results and for plotting purposes. The advantage of Python was quick processing
of several data files, in which similar calculations were needed. Furthermore, Python was able to
read and write data in various file formats, which was useful, as data acquisition software had dif-
ferent file output. Python was used to create scripts for the interpretation of BE measurements, the
calculation of the triaxial and DSS test results, as well as plotting of several data files.
3.3.2
Excel
Excel is a spreadsheet software. Excel was used for the processing of test results and plotting of
these. Excel was used in cases where a large amount of manual user interpretation was needed.
For example for calculation of damping ratio in the RC tests, or by statistical comparison of BE
results. Furthermore, Excel was used for the creation of test specifications, as well as reconstitution
specifications.
3.3.3
Maple
Maple is a commercial mathematics software that computes numerical and symbolic solutions
to mathematical problems. Maple was used for simple calculations, without large data sets, for
example the analytical solutions of FE problems.
3.3.4
ParaView
ParaView is a open-source interactive visualization software. ParaView was used for visualization
of the GeoHyTE FE models.
3.3.5
Powerpoint
Powerpoint is a presentation software, which is used to create slideshows. Powerpoint was used
for the creation of illustrations and figures in this thesis report.
3.3.6
GeoHyTE
GeoHyTE (Moldovan et al., 2024) is a Matlab based FE software, which is used for interpretation
of BE data. The software and its function was described in detail in subsection 3.6.4.
3.3.7
PicoScope
PicoScope is the oscilloscope software used in combination with a physical oscilloscope to obtain
the BE signals. The software picks up the function generator’s input signal as well as the receiver
BE signal. Mathematical expressions, such as the average of a measured signal, can be applied
in the software. PicoScope records the signals, which can then be saved on the data acquisition
25
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:36==
computer. Picoscope can also be used for interpretation of the BE signal, by the use of guiding
rulers. An example of a BE signal in PicoScope, with the FA interpreted, can be seen in Figure 3.10.
Figure 3.10: Screen capture of the PicoScope software with a BE measurement loaded. The blue

3.3.8
GDSLab
GDSLab is a control and data acquisition software developed by GDS Instruments. The software
was used to control the cell and the back pressure in the RC test setup, as well as to handle data
acquisition. GDSLab consists of two windows, when the software is launched. The first window
contains the hardware display, and the second window contains the menu in which the test setup
is controlled. In the hardware display, the user can check the status of the pressure transducers,
LVDTs, pumps, and actuators, as well as the calibration files associated with these, see Figure 3.11.
Furthermore, the hardware readings can be zeroed and controlled from this display, by clicking on
each reading.

3.3.9
GDS RCA
GDS RCA is a control and data acquisition software developed by GDS Instruments. The software
is specifically developed for the resonant and damping test in the RC setup. The software consists
of three tabs, ”Resonant Test”, ”Damping Test”, and ”Torsional Shear”. In this thesis, only the
first two tabs were used, as torsional shearing was not conducted. In the ”Resonant Test” tab,
the user has the option of performing a torsion or a flexure test. In this thesis, only the torsion
test was performed. In the ”Damping Test” tab, the user can conduct a free vibration damping
test on the test specimen. When launching GDS RCA the user is asked to confirm the amplifier
gain settings, see Figure 3.16. It is important that the gain in the software and in the amplifier is
identical. A mismatch between these will cause wrong calculations of the strains, as the velocity
of the accelerometer will deviate.
Figure 3.16: Amplifier settings controlled in GDS RCA.
30
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:41==
Once confirmation of amplifier settings has been made, the main window in GDS RCA will open,
see Figure 3.17. In this window, the torsion test parameters can be defined. However, before
defining these parameters, it is important to open ”Settings” and cross-check with the GDS RCA
user manual (GDS Instruments, 2014), if all settings are correct, as well as the specimen dimen-
sions, see Figure 3.18. In the ”Specimen” tab, the specimen height and diameter is defined. In the
”Device” tab, the accelerometer position is defined, which is described in the GDS RCA manual.
In the ”Acquisition” tab, the data points per cycle, cycles to reach full amplitude, cycles at full
amplitude, and time delay between forced vibrations are defined. Here, the standard values from
the GDS RCA user manual were used. In the ”Damping” tab, the number of seconds before the
excitation is stopped for the damping test is defined. In the ”Calibrations” tab, calibration parame-
ters for the system is defined. Most of these values are predefined in the user manual, while some
of them needs to be updated for the specific system, see subsection 3.4.6. In the ”Data File” tab,
the save directory is defined. The ”Shear” tab is only relevant for torsional shear, which was not
conducted in this thesis. In the ”Additional” tab, a soft zero can be applied to the pressure and
displacement readings.
Figure 3.17: Torsional resonant phase window in GDS RCA.
31
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:42==
Figure 3.18: Settings window in GDS RCA. Figure is from the GDS RCA user manual (GDS
Instruments, 2014).
After going through the settings, the user will go back to the main window in GDS RCA, seen in
Figure 3.17. Here, the output amplitude of the sinusoidal signal created by the function generator
can be defined. This is followed by defining either a coarse sweep or a fine sweep. For the coarse
sweep, a lower and upper value of frequencies is defined, as well as an increment. In the case of
Figure 3.17, a coarse sweep is defined from 30 Hz to 150 Hz with an amplitude of 1 V and an
increment of 5 Hz for each step. During the sweep, the accelerometer output will be plotted as
the amplitude voltage as a function of time in the graph area. When the sweep is finished, FFT is
applied to the signal, which converts the time domain into the frequency domain. This allows for
a graph containing the amplitude as a function of frequency. If the resonance frequency is applied
during the coarse sweep, a clear peak amplitude will be seen in the graph, automatically marked
by a vertical dashed line, see Figure 3.19. The dashed line can be manually shifted in case the user
disagrees with the automatic interpretation.
Figure 3.19: Graph after coarse sweep in the range 60 Hz to 110 Hz with an increment of 1 Hz and
1 V amplitude in GDS RCA. The peak amplitude was found to be at a frequency of 92 Hz, with an
amplitude of 1461.94 mV. Figure is from the GDS RCA user manual (GDS Instruments, 2014).
32
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:43==
Following a successful coarse sweep, a fine sweep can be conducted to get closer to the resonance
frequency. Here, the estimate of the resonance frequency found by the coarse sweep is automati-
cally inserted as the estimate. The user can then define a band and an increment in Hz. The band
is subtracted and added to the estimated resonance frequency to create a range of frequencies for
the sweep. The increment at the fine sweep should be smaller than for the coarse sweep, to get
closer to the resonance frequency. The fine sweep works in the same way as the coarse sweep, but
is usually conducted in smaller ranges with smaller increments. Using this procedure will signifi-
cantly reduce the time it takes to find the resonance frequency of the system. Once the resonance
frequency has been determined, a damping test can be performed at the resonance frequency. The
damping test is defined in the ”Damping Test” tab as shown in Figure 3.20.
Figure 3.20: Damping test window in GDS RCA.
The resonance frequency found by the fine sweep and the output amplitude are automatically in-
serted into the test parameters. The user can then choose the number of cycles for the calculation
of the logarithmic decrement δ. After clicking ”Start Damping Test”, the damping test will start.
The driving plate will vibrate at the given frequency and amplitude for the amount of time defined
in the ”Damping” tab in the settings window, and then stop. The output of the accelerometer will
be plotted as a function of time in the first plotting area, whereas the logarithmic of the peaks
are plotted as a function of number of cycles in the other plotting area. In the second graph, the
logarithmic decrement is found by Equation 3.43, and thereby the damping ratio can be found by
Equation 3.44.

3.3.12
Geocalib
Geocalib is Geo’s in-house calibration software. Geocalib works as a data acquisition software,
as well as a transducer verification software. Geocalib works by connecting a desired transducer
to the data acquisition system, so that transducer readings can be logged. In the first window, the
type of calibration is defined, including the ambient temperature at time of the calibration, see
Figure 3.29. Optionally, remarks about the calibration can be entered.
Figure 3.29: Calibration information window in Geocalib.
After defining the calibration type, a window for the specific type of calibration will appear, see
Figure 3.30. Here, the type of transducer, serial number, and supply voltage is entered. The supply
voltage for all transducers in this thesis was 12 V. Then, the range of the transducer is typed in,
which corresponds to the full range the transducer is able to measure. Following this, the calibration
range is entered, corresponding to the range in which the calibration is conducted. In most cases,
the calibration range is equal to the transducer’s measuring range. Furthermore, a number of steps
is defined, which calculates the step size for each measurement. For example, an LVDT with
a calibration range d ∈[0 mm, 25 mm], calibrated in 5 steps, would have a step size of 5 mm,
which means that a measurement is made every 5 mm of displacement. Finally, the reference
setup is chosen, which is a transducer of known precision and calibration factor used as reference
measurements.

factor. Then, a file is created which contains all the calibration parameters. This file can be loaded
into the ”Connections” window in Lablog, for automatic acquisition of the calibration factor. The
deviation plot, as well as the final calibration factor, can be seen in the last window of Geocalib,

3.4
Calibration of equipment
For the calibration of measuring devices, the calibration software Geocalib described in subsec-
tion 3.3.12 was used. All equipment was calibrated within a reasonable time prior to testing, either
by the author of the thesis or by a technician at Geo, using standard practice. A description of how
each type of equipment was calibrated is stated in the following section.
3.4.1
Linear variable differential transformer
The LVDTs emit an electrical signal when a needle is displaced inside the LVDT housing. The
LVDT works by an electromagnetic coupling in the LVDT, which changes based on the location
of the needle’s core. The LVDT has a range of -10 to +10 VDC (Novotechnik, 2024), which
changes linearly as the needle is displaced. The LVDTs were calibrated by fixing them so that only
the needle could displace. The needle was displaced by a Mitutoyo digital micrometer (Mitutoyo,
2024), which was used as the reference device. The Mitutoyo micrometer accurately measured the
displacement of the LVDT needle during calibration. The micrometer applied a slight displace-
ment to the LVDT needle, ensuring that it was within the linear measuring range, before zeroing the
reading. The micrometer displacement was then increased in incremental steps, within the mea-
suring range of the LVDTs. A minimum of five displacement steps were applied in the defined
measuring range. After reaching the upper range of the LVDT, the displacement was incremen-
tally lowered to the original position, ensuring linearity of the output signal in both directions of
the LVDT. This displacement process was repeated three times, ensuring repeatability of the cali-
43
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:54==
bration. A photo of an LVDT calibration setup can be seen from Figure 3.34. A similar approach
was used for calibration of the local LVDTs.
Figure 3.34: Photo of the Mitutoyo micrometer. In this case, a displacement of 21.488 mm is
applied to an LVDT.
3.4.2
Pressure transducers
The pressure transducers output an electrical signal when mechanical pressure is applied. The pres-
sure transducers were calibrated using a hydraulic deadweight tester from Budenberg (Budenberg,
2024). The hydraulic deadweight tester consisted of a liquid tank filled with hydraulic fluid which
was pushing on a piston. The pressure transducers were connected to the liquid tank through a pipe
and reference stainless steel plates were placed on the piston to apply pressure. The pressure was
applied by increasing the pressure in the system until the piston lifted the plates. Each plate rep-
resents a pressure applied to the pressure transducers. The pressure was increased incrementally,
with a minimum of five load steps in the measuring range of the transducer. The transducer was
then unloaded incrementally to the initial position, ensuring linearity in the output signal in both
loading and unloading. This loading process was repeated three times, ensuring repeatability of
the calibration. A photo of the calibration setup can be seen in Figure 3.35.
44
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:55==
Figure 3.35: Photo of the Budenberg Hydraulic Deadweight Tester. 1) Pipe for connecting the
pressure transducer to the system. 2) Disc of calibrated weight on top of pressure piston. 3) Oil
reservoir. 4) Lever which pumps oil into the system. 5) Valves for opening/closing of oil channels.
6) Schematic of the hydraulic system, with instructions of valve settings.
3.4.3
Force transducers
A force transducer consists of a metal body in which strain gauges are installed (Futek Advanced
Sensor Technology, Inc., 2024). When a force is applied at the ends of a force transducer, the trans-
ducer will deform, which causes strain in the transducer. If the applied stress is within the range of
the force transducer, removing the stress should cause the transducer to return to its original state.
The strains from the deformation are captured by the strain gauges, which will elongate or contract,
based on the loading conditions. When strain gauges are subjected to compressive strains, they will
contract, while tensile strains will elongate the strain gauges. This deformation of the strain gauges
will cause a change in the resistance of the gauges. When elongated, the resistance increases, and
when contracted, it will decrease. These resistance changes can be converted into an electrical
signal which can be measured and stored using a data acquisition system. A concept illustration
of a typical ”S-Beam” type force transducer subjected to loading can be seen on Figure 3.36.
45
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:56==
Force
Compression
Tension
Elongated strain
gauge
Relaxed strain
gauge
Contracted strain
gauge
Polymide film
Metal wire
Figure 3.36: Concept illustration of a force transducer and the strain gauges in these. When a
force is applied in the top and bottom of the transducer, the transducer will deform, contracting or
elongating the strain gauges.
Force transducers are calibrated per standard at the manufacturer, and therefore only a verification
of the calibration was conducted. This was done to ensure that the prior stress states on the trans-
ducer did not exceed the design stress range, causing permanent deformation to the transducer. The
force transducers were verified by applying a given force to the force transducer and capturing the
electrical signal in Geocalib. Geocalib was directly coupled to the force actuator of the test setup,
and the force was controlled through Geocalib. Furthermore, a reference transducer was used to
ensure that the transducer readings were identical. The reference transducer was calibrated by an
external company, which specializes in calibration. Thus, the calibration factor and precision were
known prior to the verification of the force transducer. The load on the force transducer, as well
as the reference transducer, was applied in incremental load steps in the force range of the force
transducer. A minimum of five loading steps were performed, as well as five unloading steps,
ensuring similar values in both loading and unloading. The loading process was repeated three
times, ensuring that the calibration was repeatable.
3.4.4
Pumps and actuators
The pumps and actuator from GDS Instruments, were calibrated at the manufacturer. The pumps
and actuator stresses were controlled to obtain the target stress in the pressure and force transduc-
ers in the test setup. When displacement-driven deformations were performed, the external LVDT
values were used to verify the displacement rate of the force actuator. Thus, the pumps and actua-
tors were verified using the force transducer, external LVDT, and pressure transducers as reference
transducers during testing.
3.4.5
Bender element
The BEs were verified by conducting a tip-to-tip test. The BEs were fixed with the tips being
parallel and in contact with each other. A signal was applied to the transmitter BE and the received
signal was recorded by the receiver BE. The signal was interpreted using the FA and PTP method,
to assess whether a delay in the BEs were present. The result of the tip-to-tip verification can be
seen in Appendix B.1.
46
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:57==
3.4.6
Resonant column drive system
Calibration of the RC drive system involves the determination of Io which is the mass polar mo-
ment of inertia of the RC drive system. The calibration was carried out following the calibration
procedure described in the GDS RCA user manual (GDS Instruments, 2014). As the geometry of
the system is complex, Io was found experimentally by using metal calibration bars with known
mechanical properties. The metal bars were installed as a test specimen, and the drive system was
attached to the top of the metal bars. The goal was to determine the resonant frequency in torsion
for three different calibration bars. Here, it was assumed that the metal calibration bar and the drive
system acted as a torsional pendulum with a single degree of freedom. Here, the drive system and
the attached masses were the pendulum mass and the calibration bar was the torsional spring. The
equation of motion can then be described as Equation 3.2.
ωn =
√
k
I
(3.2)
Where ωn is the natural angular frequency of the vibrating metal bar, k is the stiffness of the metal
bar and I is the mass polar moment of inertia of all masses on top of the metal bar. A linear equation
expressing the mass polar moment of inertia of the added mass can be derived as Equation 3.3.
Iam = k
ω2n
+ Io
(3.3)
When plotting Iam against 1/ω2
n, a linear regression can be made, where the intercept with the
y-axis is equal to Io. The average Io found for all the tests with different calibration bars was used
as the final Io, which was used as the mass polar moment of inertia of the RC drive system.
3.4.7
Direct simple shear setup
The ShearTrac-II actuators were calibrated by Geocomp, and verified from the data acquisition of
the force transducers and LVDTs.
3.5

The experiments in this thesis consisted of nine main triaxial tests, with BE and local strain mea-
surements, three RC tests, and six DSS tests. The test program was designed such that the density-
stress matrix seen in Equation 3.4 was obtained.

and stress, respectively. Thus, a robust framework for the tests was created.
3.5.1
Reconstitution of specimens
The test specimens were reconstituted dry, using the Ladd undercompaction method (Ladd, 1978).
Each test specimen was compacted into nt total layers, with an initial water content of 10 %.
Using the minimum and maximum dry densities of Table 3.2, and the desired relative densities
stated in Tables 3.4, 3.6 and 3.7, the undercompaction of each sand layer could be determined
from Equation 3.5.
