(3.6)
Where γn,d is the unit weight of the sand layer, hn is the height of the specimen at each layer, A
is the area of the layer, g = 9.81 m/s2 is the gravitational constant and γtarget = γs/(e0 + 1) is
the target dry density of the specimen. The dry mass of each layer mn, was found by subtracting
the previous macc,n from the current. For the RC and triaxial tests, a latex membrane was installed
inside a split compaction mold, secured at the bottom with two o-rings. A vacuum pump was con-
nected to the mold, ensuring that there were no folds in the membrane. Each layer was compacted
with a compaction hammer while each hit was counted. Afterwards, the depth from the top of the
compaction mold to the top of the layer was measured until the correct layer height was obtained.
After compaction of the specimen, the top head was installed on the specimen and leveled using a
spirit level. The membrane was then secured to the top head using two o-rings, and the split mold
was removed. For the DSS test, a latex membrane was installed inside the sliding rings, and the
specimen was similarly compacted inside this membrane. From table Table 3.3, the reconstitution
properties for each specimen type can be seen.
Table 3.3: Reconstitution properties of each specimen type.

3.5.2
Bender element measurements
BE measurements were conducted at specific test phases of the triaxial tests. When measurements
were performed, the same procedure was followed for all tests. The function generator was set to
produce one period of a signal type every 5 ms, with an amplitude of 10 V at a given frequency. The
signal was then observed in the PicoScope software, which plotted the input signal, the received
signal, and the average of the received signal. The average of the received signal was calculated,
as multiple waves were applied to the system. The frequency was increased and decreased in
increments of 1 kHz, in the range of f ∈[1 kHz, 15 kHz], while observing the amplitude of the
averaged received signal. When the frequency which yielded the highest amplitude was found,
a measurement was taken at this frequency, followed by two measurements taken at a frequency
0.5 kHz above and below this frequency. This yielded a minimum of three measurements for
each signal type. This process was carried out for the sinusoidal, ramp, and square wave signals,
described in subsection 2.2.4. When these measurements were completed, the function generator
was adjusted to produce a sine sweep. Here, the frequency was increased over a time period of
48
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:59==
T = 1 s, in a frequency range containing the same frequencies as measured from sinusoidal burst
waves.
In the test specifications shown in Appendix H, for each test, it was stated at which test stages
the BE measurements were carried out. BE measurements were performed after applying the in-
stallation pressure, after saturation and application of the back pressure and at consolidation stress
after reaching the EOP. The installation measurement was used to determine the initial Vs of the
specimen, without saturation. The measurement after saturation was performed to see how Vs
was affected by increasing the saturation of the specimen. The measurement at the consolidation
stress was performed to see how Vs was affected by an increase in stress, and therefore, also by
the compaction of the specimen.
3.5.3
Resonant column test
The RC test was conducted in accordance with the ASTM D4015-21 standard (ASTM Interna-
tional, 2021). The RC tests were conducted as fixed-base in a Stokoe type RC setup from GDS
Instruments at drained conditions. The resonance phase was conducted torsional, to obtain the
shear modulus degradation. The setup was also capable of conducting a flexural resonance phase,
however, the focus of this thesis was determining the shear modulus degradation. The flexural res-
onance phase is used to determine E, therefore, the flexural resonance phase was not carried out
to avoid causing too much unwanted deformation, which could disturb G obtained in the torsional
resonance phase. The laboratory program for the RC tests in this thesis can be seen in Table 3.4.
The tests are ordered from low to high values of the relative density Dr.

Installation
Before installation of the test specimen, a start check was performed. For the RC test, GDSLab
was used which was launched and checked to see if the correct calibration files were loaded. In
GDSLab, the pressure transducers were zeroed at the desired reference points. The test specimen
was then prepared as described in subsection 3.2.2. An installation pressure of 20 kPa was applied
in the cell, while keeping the back pressure at 1 kPa to ensure that the pump remained stable. When
the system reached equilibrium, indicated by no change in volumetric and axial strain, the axial
strain was zeroed.
Saturation
In the saturation stage, the cell pressure was increased to 35 kPa. For the tests conducted at Dr = 80
% and Dr = 90 %, the specimen was flushed with CO2 prior to saturation. CO2 flushing was
applied to these two specimens, as difficulties in reaching proper saturation were experienced. The
specimen was then saturated by flushing with de-aired tap water from the bottom of the specimen.
The pore pressure valve was opened to fill the channel with water. When steady flow of water
was observed, the valve was closed and the top valve was opened. The de-aired tap water was
flushed out through the top. When steady flow was observed, the valves were closed, and the
back pressure pump was connected to the system. A back pressure of 15 kPa was applied to the
specimen, until the pore pressure transducer showed a constant value. The top head valve was then
opened, allowing air bubbles and water to flow out of the specimen. This was repeated until no air
49
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:60==
bubbles were observed in the tubes. The pressure in the specimen was then allowed to build up to
15 kPa.
Increase of back pressure (Skempton B check)
After saturation of the specimen, the back pressure was increased to 700 kPa with a rate of 600
kPa/h. Similarly, the cell pressure was increased at the same rate, maintaining a 20 kPa effective
stress. After reaching a back pressure of 700 kPa, the system was allowed to reach equilibrium
before closing the valves. After the valves were closed, the behavior of the pore pressure was
observed. If the pore pressure continued to decrease, which means that the pore pressure could
not be kept constant, the valves were opened again and allowed to stabilize for a longer time.
However, if the back pressure decreased slightly and then stabilized, the back pressure and cell
pressure were noted, and the cell pressure was increased by 50 kPa while the back pressure valves
were kept closed. Assuming that water and the soil skeleton are incompressible, increasing the
cell pressure by 50 kPa should also increase the pore pressure by approximately 50 kPa if proper
saturation was carried out. When the pore and cell pressure stabilized, the stresses were noted
again and a Skempton B-value could be found by Equation 3.7 (International Organization for
Standardization, 2018a).
B = ∆u
∆σc
(3.7)
Where ∆u is the change in pore pressure, and ∆σc is the change in cell pressure, from the prior
stress state, to the current stress state. The B-value was defined under the same conditions as
for the triaxial test in accordance with DS/EN ISO 17892-9:2018 (International Organization for
Standardization, 2018a). Here, saturation was considered complete when a B-value of at least
0.95 was achieved. Otherwise, the cell pressure and back pressure should be increased. Due to the
limitation of the RC pressure cell, the cell pressure could not be increased further than 800 kPa,
as an effective stress of 200 kPa was needed for the consolidation stage. If no significant increase
was observed after increasing the pressure, then the saturation was incomplete. The specimen was
then unloaded, and saturation was continued. When a proper B-value was achieved, the valves
were opened to the back pressure, and the system was allowed to reach equilibrium, defined as a
constant back volume.
Consolidation
Once a proper B-value was obtained, the consolidation phase was conducted. During the con-
solidation phase, the cell pressure was increased, keeping the back pressure constant, to obtain
the desired effective stress. The cell pressure was increased at a constant rate of 300 kPa/h to
allow the pore water to dissipate. The p′ at the consolidation stage for each test can be seen in Ta-
ble 3.4. When the desired consolidation stress was reached, the test was allowed to reach the EOP.
EOP was defined under the same conditions as for the triaxial test in accordance with DS/EN ISO
17892-9:2018 (International Organization for Standardization, 2018a). Here, the EOP was reached
when the volume change is less than 0.1 % of the volume of the specimen per hour or 0.1 cm3/h,
whichever is greater.
Resonance & damping phase
After the consolidation phase the resonance & damping phase were carried out. The resonance
phase was controlled using the GDS Instruments software GDS RCA, described in subsection 3.3.9.
In the torsional testing window, the signal output amplitude in volts was entered and a coarse sweep
was conducted. In the coarse sweep, a range of f ∈[30, 300] Hz was used for most tests. An
increment of 5 Hz was chosen and the coarse sweep was performed. The system then, using the
function generator, applied an electrical sinusoidal signal to the coils at the given amplitude, while
sweeping through the range of frequencies. The electrical signal caused an electromagnetic field to
be generated in the coils, which caused a torsional excitation at the given frequency and amplitude
to the top of the specimen, see Figure 3.37.
50
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:61==
Figure 3.37: Concept illustration of the torsional excitation of the RC setup seen from above. (a)
RC setup seen from above. (b) Torsional excitation movement marked by dashed lines.
The accelerometer measured the amplitude of the response of the specimen in volts, which was
logged using the data acquisition system. When the defined frequency range had been applied, FFT
was applied to the received signal from the accelerometer, and GDS RCA plotted the amplitude as
a function of frequencies. The estimated resonance frequency fest was then determined from the
frequency at peak amplitude in the plot, as illustrated in Figure 3.19. Using fest obtained from the
coarse sweep, a fine sweep was carried out. Here, fest was used as the output frequency, while
a frequency band and increment in hertz were defined. The band was used to define a frequency
range as frange = fest ± band, creating a minimum and maximum frequency range for the fine
sweep. Then, similarly to the coarse sweep, the frequency range was applied to the system, causing
a torsional excitation. This resulted in a resonance frequency with a precision determined by the
choice of increment. In this thesis, an increment of 0.1 Hz was chosen, and the band was adjusted
as needed in the range band ∈[5 Hz, 10 Hz], as the resonance frequency decreased in greater
increments as the strains increased. In the standard D4015-21 (ASTM International, 2021) it was
stated that the test should be performed at γ ∈[10−5 %, 0.2 %]. The amplitudes to obtain this
strain range were applied following Geo’s best practice and procedure, and was assessed during
testing. The scan interval was furthermore adjusted to accommodate for larger deformations in the
specimen at the larger amplitudes. The applied output voltages for each test, as well as the scan
interval, can be seen in Table 3.5.
51
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:62==

The coarse sweep was applied at the initial measurement in all tests until a clear fest was found.
For most tests, the output voltage of 0.0005 V was too low to obtain a clear peak amplitude and
was therefore not used in the analysis. After obtaining fest, it was sufficient to apply fine sweeps,
until the verification voltage was reached. The verification measurement ensured that the soil
had deteriorated. In the verification measurements, the voltage was reduced, and therefore the
resonance frequency was expected to increase in comparison to the prior measurement. In the
case of this thesis, 0.0060 V was used for verification. This value was chosen because it was
low enough not to cause permanent deformations in the specimen but large enough to observe
a clear peak amplitude. A coarse sweep was conducted to redetermine the resonance frequency
at the verification measurement, followed by a fine sweep. The verification measurement was
then compared to the prior verification measurement to ensure that the resonance frequency was
decreased. A decrease meant that the soil had deteriorated, which was expected after applying
the torsional excitations. An increase in resonance frequency between verification measurements
indicated issues with the test setup. During testing, this was observed when the magnets came in
contact with the coils because of uneven deformation in the top of the specimen. In this case, the
test was stopped and the test was evaluated to determine if the upper limit of shear strain had been
reached, that is γ = 0.2 % as described in the standard. Otherwise, the specimen would be taken
down and a new test would be prepared.
After each fine sweep, the pore water was allowed to dissipate, followed by a damping test on the
specimen. The goal of the damping test was to obtain the viscous damping ratio D, by conduct-
ing a free vibration decay test (ASTM International, 2021). The test was conducted by applying
a continuous sinusoidal wave excitation at the same amplitude and resonance frequency as found
from the torsional fine sweep. The excitation was then stopped and the specimen was allowed to
vibrate freely. The free vibration amplitude was measured by the accelerometer as the specimen
52
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:63==
dampens. 50 cycles of free vibration damping were recorded for the calculation of the damping
ratio, ensuring sufficient cycles for representative calculations. When the damping test had been
performed and the pore water had dissipated, the next torsional measurement could begin. When
the full range of amplitudes stated in Table 3.5 was performed, the magnets were inspected to deter-
mine if there was a proper distance between the magnets and the coils. If this was the case, the next
consolidation step could be performed. In the case of magnets being too close to the coils, the test
specimen was unloaded to installation pressure, followed by applying a vacuum to the specimen to
avoid the specimen collapsing, and the magnets were adjusted. The complete test preparation pro-
cess was repeated, including installation, saturation, Skempton B check, and consolidation, before
continuing with the next resonance phase. For each RC test, three consolidation and resonance
phases were performed, as seen in Table 3.4.
Reruns
A rerun had to be performed for the RC_1 test, as can be seen from Table 3.4. This was due
to RC_3 yielding higher G values, which was not expected, due to the lower Dr. Therefore,
the corresponding BE tests were interpreted for Dr = 70 % and Dr = 80%, and it was found
that the RC_1 test deviated significantly from the BE tests. Therefore, a rerun was conducted,
which yielded more reliable values. It was uncertain what the issue with RC_1 was, however, the
inaccuracy might stem from user error, due to unfamiliarity with the test setup at the first test.
3.5.4
Triaxial test
The triaxial tests were conducted in accordance with the DS/EN ISO 17892-9:2018 standard (In-
ternational Organization for Standardization, 2018a). The triaxial tests conducted in this thesis
were conducted as consolidated isotropically drained tests (CID). In Table 3.6 the laboratory pro-
gram for each triaxial test conducted can be seen. The tests are primarily ordered by increasing
values of Dr, and secondarily by increasing values of p′. Due to setup limitations, the diameter of
the specimen was chosen as 7 cm. The choice of specimen height was based on subsection 2.4.5,
where previous research suggested a height of at least 3λ. Therefore, a specimen height of 14 cm
was chosen, which yielded a height-to-diameter ratio of H/D = 2.

Installation
Before installation of the test specimen, a start check was performed. This included evaluating
whether LVDTs, pressure transducers, pumps, and the actuator were connected to the right chan-
nels and calibration files in Lablog. The pore pressure transducer and cell pressure transducer were
53
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:64==
zeroed at a pressure equal to that of a water table at the top of the bottom pedestal. The specimens
were reconstituted using the Ladd undercompaction method (Ladd, 1978), as described in sub-
section 3.5.1. After reconstitution of the specimen, a vacuum of about 15 kPa was applied to the
specimen. The valves were closed to the specimen and using the readings from the pore pressure
transducer, it was checked if the system could keep the vacuum relatively steady. This check was
used to determine if there were leakages, either in the system or on the membrane. If a leakage
was observed, the cause would be evaluated and, if possible, corrected. Otherwise, the specimen
would be taken down and a new membrane would be installed. If no leakage was observed, the
installation of local strain LVDTs could be performed. Local strain LVDTs were installed, such
that the center of each LVDT attachment was placed at one-third of the total height of the specimen
from the top of the specimen and the bottom of the specimen, see Figure 3.38.
Figure 3.38: Concept drawing of the placement of local strain LVDTs.
The steps of attaching the local LVDTs can be seen in Figure 3.39. The LVDT attachments con-
sisted of three 3D-printed parts, which included: 1) Guide attachments, used for alignment of the
LVDT and needle, at the correct distance. 2) LVDT attachment, which housed the local LVDT.
3) The needle attachment, which contained the LVDT needle. After the guide attachments were
glued to the membrane, the distance between the centers of these was measured to ensure the cor-
rect distance for the calculation of the local strain. Then, the main attachments were glued onto
the membrane, followed by another layer of glue, which was added around the attachments and
gently spread around the area of the attachments. This helped distribute the forces in the glue,
ensuring that the LVDT attachments did not detach before reaching the shear phase. The glue was
then allowed to dry for at least 30 minutes and for up to 2 hours. As the LVDTs were connected to
a cable, the cable was adjusted so that it did not create tension in the membrane, as this could lead
to incorrect measurements or detachment of the LVDT.
54
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:65==
Figure 3.39: Installation steps for local LVDTs. (a) Guidelines were drawn onto the specimen
at H/3 from the top and bottom of the specimen. (b) Guide attachments were glued onto the
specimen. (c) LVDT and needle holder attachments were glued onto the guide attachments. (d)
The LVDT was secured in the attachment, and an o-ring was installed to increase the friction on
the LVDT.
The above steps were repeated on the side 180˚ opposite the first LVDT. Once the specimen and
LVDT installation was complete, the pressure cell was installed and the cell was filled with tap
water. The actuator was lowered until just before contact with the piston rod, and the force trans-
ducer reading was zeroed. In the case of the internal transducer, the piston rod was attached to
the actuator using three screws, followed by zeroing the force reading with the force transducer
being submerged. The external LVDT was then attached to the piston rod, with a displacement
of the LVDT of 5 mm applied, which ensured the needle was within the linear measuring area.
Afterward, the LVDT readings were zeroed. The external LVDT was displaced using an object of
known dimension, to ensure correct readings. Finally, an installation pressure of 20 kPa effective
stress was applied to the specimen, by increasing the cell pressure.
Saturation
For saturation, the cell pressure was increased to 35 kPa. The specimen was then saturated by
opening the valve at the pore pressure transducer and flushing with de-aired tap water, from the
bottom of the specimen. When steady flow of water was observed, the pore pressure valve was
closed and tightened, ensuring that there was no trapped air in the valve. The valve connected
to the top head tube was opened to allow water to flow out of the specimen. The specimen was
flushed from the bottom to the top using de-aired tap water from a bottle. When steady flow was
observed, the top head valve was closed. The back pressure pump was then connected to allow
a 15 kPa back pressure to build up inside the specimen. The top head valve was then opened to
flush out the air bubbles. This procedure was repeated until no air was observed in the tubes. The
top head valve was adjusted to allow the back pressure pump to apply pressure through the top of
the specimen. A 15 kPa back pressure was allowed to build up, until the pore pressure and back
volume were constant.
55
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:66==
Permeability test
For every Dr, a permeability test was carried out. The permeability tests were conducted in ac-
cordance with DS/EN ISO 17802-11:2019 (International Organization for Standardization, 2019).
The tests were conducted after saturation, to ensure that all pores in the specimen was filled with
water. The cell pressure was increased until an effective stress of 80 kPa was reached, assuming
a pore pressure of zero, ensuring that an increase in back pressure would not cause the effective
stress to become negative, thus collapsing the test specimen. The permeability tests were then
conducted by increasing the back pressure and allowing the pore water to seep out through the top
of the specimen. The back pressure was adjusted such that the pore pressure reached a relatively
constant value while keeping the valves open. The seeping water was allowed to flow into a water
bottle, which was placed on a scale, as seen in Figure 3.8. The scale was connected to the data
acquisition system, which allowed logging of the expelled fluid. When a back pressure yielding
a stable pore pressure was found, the expelled fluid from the pump was plotted with the expelled
fluid on the scale. It was checked that the same amount of water expelled from the pump was also
expelled onto the scale. Following the check, the test was allowed to run for at least 15 minutes,
ensuring adequate data to obtain a representative coefficient of permeability. When 15 minutes
had passed, the back pressure was decreased and the valves were closed. The volume of the back
pressure pump was assessed and filled if necessary. Then, the permeability test was repeated at
least four times at different back pressures to ensure representative values of permeability based
on different stress states.
Increase of back pressure (Skempton B check)
After the specimen was saturated or the permeability test had been conducted, the back pressure
was increased. The effective stress was kept constant at 20 kPa, by increasing the cell pressure at
the same rate as the back pressure. The back pressure was increased until it reached a back pressure
of 1200 kPa. The back pressure pump was found to be slower at times than the cell pressure pump,
resulting in an increase in effective stress beyond 20 kPa. To accommodate for this, a function in
Lablog was used, which adjusted the pump rates, so that an effective stress of 20 kPa was kept
relatively constant at all times. When a back pressure of 1200 kPa was obtained, the valves were
kept open until equilibrium was reached. Equilibrium was defined as the observation of constant
pore pressure and pump volume. Then following the same procedure as in the RC test, a Skempton
B check was conducted, see subsection 3.5.3.
Consolidation
Once a proper B-value was obtained, the consolidation phase was conducted. During the con-
solidation phase, the cell pressure was increased, to obtain the desired effective stress. The cell
pressure was increased at a constant rate of 300 kPa/h, to allow the pore water to dissipate. The p′
at consolidation for each test can be seen in Table 3.6. When the desired consolidation stress was
reached, the test was allowed to reach the EOP (International Organization for Standardization,
2018a).
Shear
When shearing the specimen, the cell pressure was kept constant, while the piston was moved into
the triaxial cell at a constant rate, applying axial compression to the test specimen. The maximum
rate of shearing was determined from DS/EN ISO 17892-9:2018 (International Organization for
Standardization, 2018a), using Equation 3.8.
vmax = Hcεaf
Ft50
(3.8)
Here Hc is the height of the specimen after consolidation, εaf is the expected axial strain at shear
failure, F is a factor based on drainage conditions, and t50 is the time needed to reach 50 % primary
consolidation. As the time to reach the EOP was short, vmax resulted in high displacement rates.
56
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:67==
Therefore, to ensure proper dissipation of the pore water, a shear rate of 1.4 mm/h was chosen for
all tests, which resulted in a rate of 1 %/h. For the first three CID tests, the specimens were sheared
until 15 % additional axial strain. However, to ensure that the tests reached the critical state, the
additional axial strain target was increased to 20 %. Furthermore, an unloading/reloading phase
was introduced for the remaining tests. These were included to assess E, G, and Poisson’s ratio ν
at unloading. The unloading steps were performed by loading the specimen in the elastic region
and then unloading before reaching the peak deviatoric stress. The specimens were unloaded to
a deviatoric stress of zero, followed by reloading. The specimen was then sheared until 20 %
additional axial strain was reached.
Reruns
Reruns had to be conducted for CID_1, CID_4, and CID_8, as can be seen in Table 3.6. For
CID_1, the LVDT attachments for the local strains were damaged, and therefore new ones had to
be created. For this reason, CID_1 was conducted without local strain measurements. However,
the BE measurements could still be used, and therefore the test was conducted to avoid time loss.
CID_4 was the first test that had an unloading/reloading phase, and the deviatoric stress was acci-
dentally allowed to reach peak, before unloading was conducted. This was due to the definition of
a Lablog function, which would automatically start the unloading phase when a specific strain was
reached. Therefore, the specimen showed soil hardening behavior and a rerun was conducted. For
the remaining tests, the shear phase was closely observed and the unloading phase was manually
started if the peak deviatoric stress was approaching. The CID_8 local strain measurements were
accidentally zeroed after starting the shear phase, which caused the shear degradation curve to be
shifted. Therefore, a rerun was performed to ensure a proper shear degradation curve.
3.5.5
Direct simple shear test
The DSS tests were conducted in accordance with the ASTM D6528−17 standard (ASTM Inter-
national, 2017). The tests were conducted in ShearTrac-II setups from Geocomp as described in
subsection 3.2.4, under constant stress conditions, corresponding to drained conditions. Two sets
of DSS tests were performed, one using the original procedure according to the standard, and one
where CO2 flushing prior to saturation was performed. The laboratory program for the DSS tests
can be seen from Table 3.7. The tests are ordered by increasing p′.

Installation
Before the specimen was installed, a start check was performed. The membrane was checked for
leakages by stretching the membrane and checking for holes before reconstituting the specimen
using the Ladd undercompaction method (Ladd, 1978). Then following the procedure described
in subsection 3.2.4 the test specimen was prepared. For the DSS tests, the DSS Shear software
from Geocomp was used. In the software, the dimensions of the specimen were entered, as well as
the test specifications. This included a definition of the consolidation phase, shear phase, and the
scan interval at each stage. A vertical installation pressure equivalent to 5 kPa was applied to the
first set of DSS and 10 kPa was applied to the second set. The difference was due to the appliance
of a back pressure in the second set, which will be further described in the saturation section.
57
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:68==
Saturation
The first DSS set was saturated by filling the water container with tap water, allowing the water
to flow freely into the specimen. This method did not result in full saturation and therefore a new
set of DSS tests was conducted, where the saturation process was improved. The specimen was
first flushed with CO2 for 30 minutes at a pressure of 5 kPa, followed by flushing with de-aired tap
water. The procedure included an initial flushing of the water container using CO2, to remove most
of the air from the container. In the top head, two holes were connected to the specimen, as well as
one hole in the bottom plate, allowing water to flow freely. The specimen was first flushed through
one of the top head holes, pushing most of the air out. Then the other hole was obstructed which
flushed CO2 through the specimen in one direction. After 15 minutes of continuous CO2 flushing,
the obstruction and CO2 tube were switched so that CO2 was flushed through the other side of the
specimen. During flushing of the second hole, de-aired tap water was poured into the container,
ensuring that air would not replace the CO2 once flushing was finished. After 30 minutes, the CO2
tube was disconnected, and while keeping one entrance obstructed, a de-aired tap water bottle was
connected directly to the specimen and kept at a height of approximately 1 meter, allowing a back
pressure to build up. Finally, the obstruction was removed, and flushing with de-aired tap water
was continued until no bubbles were observed on the water surface.
Consolidation
The consolidation was conducted in doubling incremental loading steps, starting from a vertical
pressure of 12.5 kPa, followed by doubling the stress until the desired consolidation stress was
reached. Each consolidation step was set to a duration of 60 minutes, to ensure that all steps
reached the EOP and to ensure the same duration of time of each stress state.
Shear
The shear phase was conducted by displacing the bottom plate in the specimen while keeping the
top head fixed. The strain rate at which the bottom plate was displaced was determined so that
the time until specimen failure was twice the time for 90 % consolidation, tf = 2t90. Since all
consolidation steps reached EOP in less than one minute, a shear strain rate of 5 % per hour was
used, as suggested by the standard (ASTM International, 2017). The test was considered finished
when 30 % shear strain was reached.

For the interpretation of BE tests, two Python scripts were written. As several measurements were
conducted at different frequencies to find the resonance frequency, a script was created to compare
the signals at different frequencies. For each frequency, up to 32 waveforms were recorded con-
taining the time step, the input signal amplitude, the received signal amplitude, and the averaged
received amplitude. When the script is launched, a window opens in which the user can browse for
a data folder containing the BE measurements. The script will then load all the waveforms from
each frequency and calculate the average of each parameter in the waveform data file. If less than
32 waveforms are available, a warning is displayed in case the user did not upload all data files.
Then, the maximum amplitude of each averaged signal is found by Equation 3.23.
Amax = Vmax −Vmin
2
(3.23)
Here, Vmax is the maximum voltage of the signal, and Vmin is the minimum voltage of the signal.
Then each signal is normalized by division with Amax and plotted in the same graph. The script
then compares each signal’s Amax, and prints the signal frequency with the highest amplitude at
the top of the graph. The graph is then saved in the folder that contains the signal measurements.
An example of a comparison plot can be seen in Figure 3.40.
Figure 3.40: Example of comparison of four BE signals conducted at the consolidation step of
CID_1.
In this example, the signal with the highest amplitude had a frequency of 6 kHz. If more time was
available, it was planned to combine this script with the BE interpretation script, which is described
below.
60
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:71==
The second script created for the interpretation of BE results was used for the interpretation of the
received signals and the calculation of Vs and G0. When the script is launched, the user is asked
to choose a data file. This data file could be based on the result of the comparison script. After
choosing a data file containing the desired signal, the data is loaded into the script, and a new
window is shown. In this window the user has several options, see Figure 3.41.
Figure 3.41: Interpretation window in BE interpretation script.
The first two input cells in the window can be used for correction of drift in the horizontal and
vertical directions. In cases of high drift at the beginning of the signal, GeoHyTE can have issues
converging to a final solution. Therefore, a drift correction function was implemented in the BE
script, for simple drift correction. If a significant amount of drift is observed, it can be corrected
such that in the beginning of the signal a flat line is created, see Figure 3.42.
Figure 3.42: Signal containing drift, which was corrected. a) Original signal containing drift. b)
Corrected signal. The signal was corrected up until the blue dashed line.
61
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:72==
Here, the horizontal threshold was set at Areceived = 0.00256 V, and the vertical threshold was
set at t = 0.55 s, illustrated by the light blue dashed lines. As a correlation window is chosen in
GeoHyTE it was deemed unnecessary to remove drift after the apparent arrival of waves in the
signal. The correlation window in GeoHyTE is explained in more detail in subsection 3.6.4.
The third input cell consists of the ρd of the specimen in the installation phase. The ρ of the spec-
imen is automatically calculated assuming Sr =100 %, a grain density ρg = 2.66 g/cm3, and a
cylindrical specimen of dimensions H = 14 cm and D = 7 cm. The fourth input cell consists of
the current axial strain, equal to εa observed at the time of the BE measurement. The εa is used
for the correction of the height of the specimen, to determine the travel distance of the waves, see
Equation 3.19. The BE script automatically utilizes both travel distance equations, when calculat-
ing Vs and G0. The fifth input cell is the upper limit on the x-axis. As the signal is shifted to start
at t = 0 s, the lower limit is automatically set to x = t = 0 s. This limit is used to scale the signal,
which eases interpretation. The sixth to ninth input cell is used for interpretation in the time do-
main. Here, the four characteristic interpretation points in Figure 2.6 can be defined. If a value is
set equal to 0.0, the interpretation will not be shown in the plotting area. For the FA methods, first
sharp, first bump and zero crossing, the travel time is found as the time of the interpretation, corre-
sponding to the time between the beginning of the input signal and the interpretation point. For the
PTP method, the script finds the time of peak amplitude in the input signal automatically, which
is subtracted from the interpreted PTP time. The travel time found by the above-mentioned inter-
pretations is then used with the travel distances Ltt and L60 to determine Vs using Equation 3.18.
Furthermore, using ρ and the found Vs, G0 can be determined using Equation 3.22. The G0 for
each interpretation method using Ltt is shown in the legend of the plot. To apply the entered values
on the plot, the ”Plot” button is clicked at the bottom of the window, and the plot will be updated
in Python.
After interpretation of the signal, the user simply clicks the ”Continue” button and the script outputs
the plot of the received signal with the arrival time interpretations. As an example, the interpretation
plot for CID_1 can be seen in Figure 3.43. In addition, two files are created in the data folder. The
first of the two output files contains the interpreted signal data, as a text file (txt), which GeoHyTE
can use as the input file. The second file is a comma separated value file (csv), with the entered
axial strain, dry density, as well as the interpreted Vs and G0 values for each interpretation method
and travel distance. In this thesis, inspiration for interpretation characteristics were taken from
Figure 2.6. The first bump in the received signal was assumed to be the P-wave arrival. After
the assumed P-wave arrival, the first S-wave arrival was assumed to be found by first sharp, as
this must be the point at which the BE starts moving, thereby being displaced by the S-wave. The
immediate peak after the first sharp was assumed to be the first peak point, which was used for the
PTP method.
62
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:73==
Figure 3.43: CID_1 Interpretation at the Consolidation phase, conducted at Dr = 80 %.
If more time was available, several factors could be added to improve the script. It was planned
to implement the CC method to include an objective interpretation method for comparison. Fur-
thermore, since the GeoHyTE input file was created from the script, it was planned to implement
automatic data transfer from Python to GeoHyTE, to create a better flow for data processing. The
script was specifically constructed for the sand used in this thesis, which means the grain density,
dimensions of the specimen, and the BE height were predefined. For a more general purpose of the
script, it was desired to implement input cells for these parameters in the interpretation window.
This could be implemented by having an initial window that contains only the soil parameters and
dimensions, followed by the interpretation window.
The scripts used for comparison and interpretation of the BE measurements can be seen in Appen-
dices K.2 and K.3.
3.6.4
GeoHyTE
GeoHyTE utilizes minimal user input, consisting primarily of soil parameters, specimen dimen-
sions, and signal properties. The software computes a FE model, resulting in a simulated received
signal of the receiver BE. The CC between the FE output signal and the experimental output signal
is found to determine the small strain shear modulus G0, as described in subsection 2.3.4. From
this point on, the CC method refers to the cross-correlation in GeoHyTE, with the above-mentioned
definition. The structure of the GeoHyTE graphical user interface (GUI) can be seen in Figure 3.44.
63

==PAGE:74==

Figure 3.44: GUI structure of GeoHyTE adapted from Moldovan et al. (2024).
In GUI 1, the user imports the BE measurement data from a BE test, and a plot will show the
received signal, see Figures 3.45 and 3.47. The user will then input the mixture density, as well as
the travel distance between the BEs. This is measured as Ltt as shown in Figure 2.5. The distance is
calculated by the height of the specimen, correcting for the current axial strain, and subtracting the
height of the two BEs, see Equation 3.19. The input section of GUI 1 can be seen in Figure 3.46.
The user will then choose a correlation window in the time interval t ∈[t0, tend] and an initial
interpretation of the shear wave arrival time. In this time interval, the received signal and the
numerical output signal will be cross-correlated. A screen capture of the signal with interpretation
can be seen in Figure 3.47.
Figure 3.45: Experimental signal loading section of GUI 1 in GeoHyTE.
64
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:75==
Figure 3.46: Material and setup specific parameters section in GUI 1 in GeoHyTE.
Figure 3.47: Graphing section of GUI 1 with interpretation of correlation window and first arrival.
The choice of initial arrival time has an influence on the amount of iterations needed for the solution
to converge. Moldovan et al. (2024) assessed the robustness to an erroneous initial estimate of S-
wave arrival. It was found that the solution would converge within 4 iterations, even when the
arrival time was significantly underestimated. In this thesis, the arrival time was chosen as the first
arrival after the assumed near-field effects. Based on the input of the user, GeoHyTE will calculate
an initial value of G0 using Equations 3.18 and 3.22. The user can then choose to save the model
as a Matlab file, as GeoHyTE saves the model implicitly, but overwrites the prior file if a new test
is conducted, see Figure 3.48.
65
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:76==
Figure 3.48: Saving and loading of GeoHyTE data in GUI 1.
In GUI 2, the user enters soil specific parameters, as well as algorithmic data, see Figures 3.49
to 3.51. Here, the user will choose between the regular or non-regular mesh option. In this thesis,
a non-regular mesh was chosen, to obtain a finer mesh, and thereby more precise calculations
in the FE model. The number of Gauss points, which are used for the boundary integrations,
and number of plotting points, which controls the resolution of the solution, are also chosen by
the user. Insufficient Gauss points will lead to larger errors in the model, while increasing them
will increase the computational time. The user can activate parallel processing, which allows
the software to utilize all processor cores in the machine for calculations of the time-discretized
problems. Furthermore, a least norm solvers option is available for computation, however, it was
not used during this thesis. Finally, the user will, if desirable, be able to change edges order, loops
order, daubechies family, daubechies refinement, and number of dyadic points. In this thesis, the
same values used in the GeoHyTE user manual (GeoHyTE User Manual, 2022) was chosen. The
algorithmic definitions used for the analysis in this thesis are stated in Table 3.8.

Where G0 is initially estimated from the input in GUI 1, and updated at the end of each iteration,

α = 1 −Kd
Ks

Where Kd is the bulk modulus of the dry porous material and Ks is the bulk modulus of the solid
grain. Assuming that the solid grain is much less compressible than the dry porous material (Ks ≫
Kd), Equation 3.25 approaches 1. Under this assumption, M can be found in Equation 3.26.
M = Kw
nw
(3.26)
Where Kw = 2.1 GPa (Shashi Menon, 2011) is the fluid bulk modulus of water, and the volume
fraction of fluid is found by nw = e0/(1 + e0). ρ is found by assuming Sr = 100 % at consoli-
dation, and ρw = 1000 kg/m3. The tortuosity τ describes the ratio between the path traveled by
water molecules and the shortest possible distance. The fluid was assumed to flow in relatively
straight lines, resulting in τ = 1. κ was found by performing soil permeability tests as described
in subsection 3.5.4. In Table 3.9 the soil input parameters for each relative density can be seen.

After GUI 2, the user will go to GUI 3, if a regular mesh was chosen, or to the mesh geometry and
definition window if a non-regular mesh is chosen. In this thesis, all models were created using a
non-regular mesh, to increase the number of element, and thereby increasing the accuracy of the
model. However, this will also increase the computational time. A typical hybrid-Trefftz element
used in the GeoHyTE mesh is illustrated in Figure 3.52.
Figure 3.52: Illustration showing terminology when defining a FE mesh.
The mesh is drawn by the user, using the Matlab Partial differential equation toolbox (pdetool)
and should replicate the test setup used to obtain the BE measurements. In this thesis, the mesh
consisted of the triaxial specimen, with the transmitter BE located at the bottom of the specimen.
The geometries were drawn using the Cartesian coordinate system, with the BE aligned so that the
swinging motion occurs in the x-direction. The user can change the height and width of the setup
elements and then change the coordinates of the lower left corner, to ensure proper alignment.
In the case of this thesis, the specimen was drawn, with the vertical center at x = 0, using the
height H and width W as stated in Table 3.10. Then, the transmitter BE was drawn in, with the
vertical center at x = 0, and the height Hb and width Wb. The transmitter BE geometry was then
68
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:79==
subtracted from the specimen geometry because the BE is not part of the sand specimen. Then,
the meshing parameters were entered. The most important parameters are the maximum edge size,
corresponding to the largest element edge length, and the mesh growth rate, which defines the rate
at which the mesh size increases away from small parts of the geometry. The maximum edge size
was found to be sufficient at 0.02 m. The mesh growth rate could be chosen between 1 and 2,
where at 1 the mesh is generated according to the finest region, which would be at the transmitter
BE, and 2 means the fastest possible growth rate away from the BE. As the mesh is very fine at the
BE, 1 would cause a lot of computational time, and therefore 1.999 was chosen. This was due to
the pdetool in Matlab not supporting the exact value of 2, and the desire for the elements to grow
as fast as possible. The mesh can then be generated and refined until satisfied. In this thesis, it
was found that refining the mesh once delivered a high resolution in the FE model, without too
long computational time. A visual representation of the mesh can be seen in Figure 3.53, and the
geometry parameters can be seen from Table 3.10.
Table 3.10: Geometric parameters of the mesh.

Figure 3.53: GeoHyTE mesh used in this thesis. The mesh was finer around the transmitter BE at

As it can be seen, the software automatically increased the number of elements close to the smaller
definitions (in this case, the transmitter BE) and then quickly increased the size of each element
69
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:80==
away from the BE. After designing the mesh, the user can save the mesh and then export it to
GeoHyTE. The mesh can then be loaded and reused in other tests that have the same test setup.
Once the mesh has been exported, the user is directed to GUI 3. In GUI 3, the external boundary
types can be defined in the mesh, in the edges of the elements. The boundary types included in
GeoHyTE are Neumann Γσ, Dirichlet Γu, Robin Γr and absorbing Γa. The exterior boundaries Γ
on the biphasic medium Ωcan be seen from Figure 3.54, and each of their purposes are described
below (Moldovan et al., 2024):
• Neumann boundaries γσ are where the components of traction tΓ are described. The traction
includes the forces applied on the solid phase, as well as the pore pressure.
• Dirichlet boundaries Γu are where the components of displacement uΓ are described. The
displacements are described as the solid phase displacements, as well as fluid seepage.
• Robin boundaries Γr are the elastic boundaries, where flexibility coefficients are defined to
enforce a linear relationship between displacement and stresses.
• Absorbing boundaries Γa absorb the incoming waves, which can approximate the impedance
difference between the specimen and the testing apparatus to zero. This will prevent wave
reflections.
Figure 3.54: Domain, Neumann, Dirichlet, Robin and absorbing boundary conditions, adapted
from the GeoHyTE user manual (GeoHyTE User Manual, 2022).
In this thesis, the Dirichlet boundaries were applied to all elements, as the displacement caused by
the wave propagation was of interest. Dirichlet boundaries were therefore defined for all elements
in GUI 3, see Figure 3.55.
70
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:81==
Figure 3.55: Definition of external boundary types in GUI 3.
In GUI 4, the user will define the boundary conditions at the edges of the mesh. In the GUI 4
window, the user will see a representation of the defined mesh, as well at a section for defining the
Dirichlet, Neumann, and Robin boundaries, see Figure 3.56. GeoHyTE will automatically calcu-
late the stiffness of the absorbing boundaries based on the mechanical parameters of the material.
71
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:82==
Figure 3.56: Definition of the external Dirichlet boundaries in GUI 4.
The Dirichlet boundary conditions are defined by specifying the normal and tangential displace-
ment in the solid phase, as well as the normal fluid seepage. In addition, for the transmitter BE
boundary conditions, the time variation is defined. The time variation is used for boundaries that
vary in time and space, which applies to the transmitter BE, as it creates the swinging motion.
The time variation of the boundary fields can be specified as analytical expressions of time, us-
ing a mathematical expression that is interpretable by Matlab. In the case of discontinuous time
variations (for example, in the case of a burst signal), the time variation can be described using
logical operations. GeoHyTE will then automatically generate an analytical expression using a
polynomial of degree D, based on the values in the field in D + 1 equally spaced points along
the boundary. Using NaN in the tangential field, the user can define sliding supports, where the
material can move freely in the tangential direction.
For all edges except those covering the transmitter BE, the boundary conditions were set to 0
in the normal direction, as well as for seepage. In the tangential direction, free movement was
allowed and therefore NaN was used. The soil specimen was assumed to be completely static and,
therefore, the time variation was set to 1. For the edges of the transmitter BE, the normal direction
varied with time. It was assumed that BE deformation could be described using a parabolic function
starting at the origin of an x-y coordinate system, and the maximum deformation was found at the
maxima of the parabolic function. The resulting function with these properties can be seen in
72
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:83==
Equation 3.27.
ux(t) = −(t −1)2 + 1
,
t ∈[0s, 1s]
(3.27)
This function was applied on the left and right edges of the transmitter BE. In GeoHyTE using the
mesh in Figure 3.53, four edges were used to describe the motion of the transmitter BE on each
side. The four edges consisted of straight lines, with a partial deformation determined by ux(t).
The y-coordinates of the edges were known due to these being placed Hb/4 apart from each other.
The horizontal displacement coordinates were defined using ux(t), solving for t. At each edge,
three nodes were defined in which the movement was described using the time variation. The first
and third nodes were placed at the ends of the edge, which means that the elements would share
their end nodes with the next edge. The second node would therefore be placed exactly in the
middle of the two nodes on the edge, meaning it could be found by the average of the coordinates
of the end nodes. An illustration of the BE movement can be seen in Figure 3.57, while detailed
calculations can be seen in Appendix J.1.
Figure 3.57: Concept illustration of BE movement. (a) Assumed analytical deformation shape,
using a parabolic definition. (b) Corresponding BE definition in FE model. Red dots symbolize
the nodes in each edge.
As the GeoHyTE model does not use the global Cartesian referential, the values in the normal
direction on the left edges are positive and the right side is negative. Applying this to the mesh
in Figure 3.53 will yield the coordinates of the nodes as described in Table 3.11. The horizontal
displacement started at 0 and ended at unity. For each edge, the displacement of the three nodes
was stated, separated by a space.

-0.13 -0.07 0.00
73
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:84==
The time variation of the lateral motion of the BE was for the burst signals described as one period
of the given wave type with an amplitude of 1 µm, followed by silence. The time variation for a
sinusoidal burst signal can be seen by Figure 3.58.
Figure 3.58: Time variation for a sinusoidal burst signal, adapted from the GeoHyTE user manual
(GeoHyTE User Manual, 2022).
This function was created using a sinusoidal function dependent on time t, with input parameters
consisting of amplitude A and frequency f, see Equation 3.28.
ux(t) = A sin(2πft)
,
t ∈[0, T]
(3.28)
As described earlier, the expression needs to be written in a way which Matlab can handle. This
includes logical statements, to handle the range of t. Taking CID_1 with a sinusoidal burst signal in
the consolidation phase as an example, f = 6000 Hz, A = 1 · 10−6 m and T = 1/f = 1.67 · 10−4
s, will by insertion in Equation 3.28 yield ”1.e-06*sin(37699.11*t)*(t<=1.67e-04)”. Here, the last
parentheses ensure that the values of t above the first period T are not included. An example for
the calculation of the time variation can be seen in Appendix J.2. The time variation was applied
to both sides of the BE edges as well as to the top edges. Furthermore, the top edge can be set
equal to NaN or 1 in the tangential direction. The only difference is that NaN will apply rolling
supports, to allow the top edges to move with the side edges, while 1 will move the top edges to the
same position as the sides. In this thesis, 1 was chosen in the tangential direction for consistency.
After applying the boundary conditions, GUI 5 is opened. Here, a visual representation of the
mesh will be shown. In this window, the user can see the defined mesh, with the numbering of all
elements and edges, as well as the normal, tangential, and seepage boundary conditions defined by
the user. An example of CID_1 can be seen from Figure 3.59.
74
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:85==
Figure 3.59: GUI 5: Visual representation of the mesh. (a) Tangential boundary conditions in the
full mesh. (b) Normal boundary conditions in the transmitter BE.
In Figure 3.59(b), the variation in the normal direction can be seen from the bottom of the trans-
mitter BE to the top.
Finally, the user is directed to GUI 6. GUI 6 works as the runtime interface, where plots of the
normalized output signals and the cross-correlation can be seen for each iteration. In this window,
the user will define the coordinates of the tip of the receiver BE (in Cartesian referential), the
direction of the receiver BE motion, the correlation window time, the correlation type, as well as
the G0 error tolerance to stop the execution. The receiver BE coordinates were found by adding
Hb to the initial defined tip-to-tip distance Ltt. The direction of the receiver motion is based on
the cosines of the direction the receiver BE is expected to vibrate in. In this case, it was expected
to vibrate in the positive x-direction, therefore the input value 1. The correlation window time
is automatically set equal to the one defined in GUI 1. The correlation type can be either zero
lag or Pearson, depending on if the correlation is performed without or with signal normalization,
respectively. In this case, the zero lag was used. And at last, the G0 tolerance was set to 0.01,
meaning that when a deviation of G0 of less than 1 % between two iteration was obtained, the
calculations are concluded. The input parameters explained above can be seen in Figure 3.60, the
GUI 6 window after finalizing a run can be seen in Figure 3.61.
75
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:86==
Figure 3.60: GUI 6 input section.
Figure 3.61: GUI 6: Runtime window of GeoHyTE after succesful execution.
In the window is a plot of the normalized output signal, consisting of the FE signal (blue), as well
as the experimentally obtained signal (black). Furthermore, a plot of the FE signal with markings
of the points of highest correlation between the simulated signal and the experimental signal is
shown. The time between these two points is τ, which corresponds to the time lag between the
FE and the experimental signal, using the CC method. In addition, Pearson correlation, zero-lag
76
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:87==
correlation, best correlation for the chosen correlation type, and time lag for highest correlation
(τ) are given. In the bottom of the window, a log is shown, where each iteration’s results are
shown. This includes the estimated G0, the estimated arrival time, the relative error, and the time
to complete the iteration. The number of iterations, as well as the total time of each test, can be
seen from Table 4.1 in subsection 4.2.2.
The cross-correlation between the experimental signal and the FE signal is described using Equa-
tion 3.29.
χ(G0, τ) =
N
∑
i=1
E(ti) × S(G0, ti+τ)
||E|| × ||S||
(3.29)
Here, E(ti) is the experimental signal at time step ti, i = 1, 2, ..., N, S(G0, ti+τ) is the simulated
signal from the FE model, utilizing G0 from the prior iteration step. GeoHyTE thereby takes the
initially determined G0 from GUI 1, calculates S(G0, ti+τ) based on this value, and cross correlates
it to E(ti). The arrival time of the shear wave is then shifted using τ found by cross-correlation at
the current iteration, and G0 is updated based on the new arrival time. This updated value of G0
is based on the update function in Equation 3.30.
ϑ(G0) =
L2
ttρ
(
Ltt
√
ρ
G0 + τmax
)
(3.30)
The update function takes G0 from the previous iteration, back calculates the prior arrival time,
adds the new time lag τmax, and then calculates the new G0, which is compared to the prior G0.
If the error is larger than the defined tolerance, a new FE simulated signal is created based on the
new G0. This procedure is repeated until the tolerance is reached and the final G0 is found.
3.6.5
Resonant column tests
The RC test results were calculated in accordance with ASTM D4015-15 (ASTM International,
2021).
Stresses and strains
The corrected height was found by subtracting the axial strain from the original specimen height,
using Equation 3.31.
Hc = H0(1 −εa)
(3.31)
Furthermore, the volume was corrected by subtracting the change in volume from the original
volume, see Equation 3.32.
Vc = V0 −∆Vc
(3.32)
The axial strain was calculated as the change in height over the initial height as Equation 3.33.
εa = ∆H
H0
(3.33)
The volumetric strain was calculated as the change in volume over the initial volume, see Equa-
tion 3.34.
εv = ∆V
V0
(3.34)
As no axial force transducer was installed in the test setup, the axial stress was assumed to be equal
to the radial stress with the addition of the driving plate (≈3 kPa). The effective stresses were
calculated by subtracting the measured pore pressure from the stresses, as seen in Equations 3.35
and 3.36.

And the deviatoric stress was found by Equation 3.38.

(3.38)
Accounting for driving system
For the fixed-free RC system, Equation 3.39 was applicable.
I/Io = β tan(β)
(3.39)
Here β was a deduced from Io found by calibration, and the specimen rotational inertia about axis
of rotation I described as I = Md2/8. Here, M is the mass of the fully saturated specimen and d
is the diameter of the specimen.
Torsional resonance phase
The shear wave velocity was found from the resonance frequency using Equation 3.40.
Vs = 2πfnH

(3.40)
Here, fn is the resonance frequency. The shear modulus was found by Equation 3.41.

(3.42)
Here Amax is the peak amplitude of the torsional resonance phase and R is the radius of the spec-

3.6.6
Triaxial tests
The triaxial test results were calculated in accordance with DS/EN ISO 17892-9:2018 (Interna-
tional Organization for Standardization, 2018a).
Stresses and strains
Most stresses and strains in the triaxial tests were calculated similar to those stated in subsec-
tion 3.6.5, with a few additions and differences. As two local axial strain LVDTs were used, the
average of these were found by Equation 3.45.
εa,local = εa1 + εa2
2
(3.45)
The local strain measurements for each LVDT were compared to ensure that there were no large
deviations. If large deviations were found, external LVDT calculations were used instead. The
radial strain was found by Equation 3.46.
εr = εv −εa
2
(3.46)
During shearing the area was corrected, for the stress to be calculated correctly, see Equation 3.47.
Acor = 1 −εv

Ac
(3.47)

And the shear stress was found by Equation 3.50.

The critical state parameter was found by the initial void ratio subtracted by the void ratio at CSL,
see Equation 3.52. The CSL was determined by the last data points in the shear phase, under the
assumption that constant volume was reached.
Ψ = e0 −eCSL
(3.52)

E = dq

The data processing of the triaxial tests were conducted using Python scripts. The scripts used the
location of the data file as input and automatically loaded the data file from the location of the file,
performed the calculations, and plotted the graphs. A secondary input file was created and placed
in each data folder. The input file contained e0, the distance between the local strain LVDTs and
their needle attachment, indexing of test phases, and interpretations of specific soil parameters.
This file was loaded by the script for the corresponding test and the stored data was used as input.
After calculations and plotting of graphs, the Python script outputs data files that were used to plot
the grouped graphs, as well as the resulting soil parameters. These included the consolidation and
shear phases, as well as the shear degradation curve. The Python scripts used for the triaxial tests
can be seen in Appendices K.5 and K.6.
3.6.7
Permeability
The permeability tests results were calculated in accordance with DS/EN ISO 17892-11:2019 (In-
ternational Organization for Standardization, 2019). The coefficient of permeability k was found
by Equation 3.61.
k =
Ql
A∆h
(3.61)
Where Q is the flow rate, l is the distance between measuring points 1 and 2 in the direction of the
flow (in this case, the specimen height), A is the cross-sectional area of the specimen and ∆h is
the difference in head of water between measuring points 1 and 2. Here, Q = ∆Vout/∆t, where
∆Vout is the volume of the outflow during the test phase, and ∆t is the time during the phase.
∆Vout could be found by the water displaced to the scale, and the temperature dependent density
of water ρw. The ρw was found from interpolation of ρw at different temperatures, see Table 3.12
∆h was found by converting the pore pressure to meters of water mH2O. Here, the conversion
from kPa to meters of water column mH2O was found from ρw and g, see Equation 3.62.

(3.62)

∆h = 0.102mH2O · u

3.6.8
Direct simple shear tests
The DSS tests are calculated in accordance with ASTM D6528-17 (ASTM International, 2017).
Stresses and strains
The εa and σa were found similarly to the triaxial tests, using Equation 3.33 and Equation 3.48,
respectively. As shear was conducted by horizontal displacement of the bottom pedestal, γ was
found by Equation 3.64.
γ = δh
Hc
(3.64)
Where δh is the horizontal displacement of the bottom pedestal. Furthermore, the shear stress was
determined by Equation 3.65.
τ = Fh
A0
(3.65)
Here, Fh is the measured horizontal force. Finally, G was determined by Equation 3.65.
G = δτ
δγ
(3.66)
The stresses and strains described in this section were automatically calculated by DSS Shear, and
were therefore verified using these equations to ensure correct calculations. A Python script was
used for plotting of the DSS test results.

4
Results
4.1
Full shear degradation curves
The full shear degradation curves were created by combining the corresponding individual shear
degradation curves from each test. The degradation curves were plotted by grouping tests with
the same Dr, see Figures 4.1 to 4.3, as well as by grouping tests with the same p′, see Figures 4.4
to 4.6. For some plots, it was not possible to obtain a shear degradation curve from local strains,
and therefore the external LVDT was used instead. These tests were marked in the figure legends
by subscript ”ext”. Furthermore, the grouped plots were normalized by p′ by dividing G of each
individual test by p′ obtained at the beginning of the shear phase of the specific test. Hence, RC G
was divided by p′ found at the beginning of the torsional phase, BE G0 and CID G were divided
by p′ found at the beginning of the shear phase in the triaxial test, and DSS G was divided by p′
at the beginning of the shear phase. Normalization was also carried out by dividing G for all tests
with G0,RC which was the G0 found for the corresponding RC test. Normalized plots can be seen
in Appendix A.

The shear degradation curves for each test were grouped by relative densities Dr = 70 %, 80 %,
and 90 %.

4.2.1
GeoHyTE finite element models
For each consolidation test, the sinusoidal burst signal at the resonance frequency was analyzed
using the GeoHyTE FE model. For each model, three plots were created, with an example shown
in Figure 4.7. Here, the horizontal displacement fields Ux were plotted in different dyadic time
steps. The first plot shows how the waves were initially generated and the second plot shows the
first arrival of the P-waves. The P-waves had negative magnitude (blue in the plots), due to P-
wave displacement fields being negative. The third plot shows the arrival of the S-waves, which
had positive magnitudes (red in the plots).

FE method was not used for repeatability assessment due to time constraints.

4.4
Triaxial testing
The triaxial test data was processed by the equations in subsection 3.6.6, showcased in tables, and
plotted in figures. The main figures and tables used in the analysis can be found in this section,
while the remaining results can be found in Appendix E. In the legends, each test is defined by Dr
and p′ conducted at the test. Some tests are marked with an ”R”, which indicates a rerun.
4.4.1
Failure patterns
After testing, two failure patterns were observed, see Figure 4.30.
Figure 4.30: Shear failure patterns observed for the triaxial tests. (a) Two shear planes (dilatancy
failure). (b) Bulging of specimen.
The two shear planes failure pattern was observed for all tests conducted at Dr = 80 % and
Dr = 90 %, while the bulging failure pattern was observed for all tests conducted at Dr = 70 %.

The triaxial tests were plotted by τ as a function of γ, see Figures 4.31 to 4.33.
