5
Analysis and discussion
5.1
Deviation in test results
Deviations in the tests resulted in the need to rerun the tests, as well as some test results being
completely removed from the analysis. For the reruns conducted at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90
%, the test setup was different from that of the original tests, which for some parameters resulted
in deviations. This included BE signals, the peak envelope, and the CSL envelope. Furthermore,
for CID_1_rerun conducted at Dr = 80 % and p′ = 100 kPa, an unloading/reloading phase
was introduced in the shear phase, which increased ψmax and Ψ as can be seen in Tables 4.17
and 4.18. For CID_6 conducted at Dr = 90 % and p′ = 200 kPa, εv had a rapid increase in the
unloading/reloading phase. This caused εr calculations to be unreliable, thus causing γ and G to
be unreliable. Therefore, this test was not included in the shear degradation curves.
5.2
Shear degradation curves
Shear degradation curves were constructed by combining the BE G0 and the shear degradation
curves for the RC, CID and DSS tests. Here, they were plotted grouped by Dr and p′, to compare
the behavior of G when p′ increased and Dr increased, respectively. The plots can be seen in
section 4.1.
5.2.1
Equipment precision
In the beginning of the test phase, a 25 kN external force transducer was used, to acquire the axial
stress throughout the triaxial tests. However, issues with extreme values were found for most of the
tests. Therefore, the shear degradation curve obtained from the external LVDT was compared to the
curves obtained from the local LVDTs. The external LVDT degradation curve was found to show
relatively reliable results after γ = 10−2 %, compared to the local LVDTs. However, at strains
smaller than this, extreme values were also observed. On the basis of this observation, recalibration
of the local LVDTs was conducted. After recalibration of the local strain LVDTs extreme values
were still observed. Therefore, raw data were inspected to assess whether the scan interval defined
in Lablog was too low to properly measure shear degradation. Here, it was found that the force
measurements had a significantly lower precision than the local LVDTs. This, combined with the
observation of the external LVDT curve showing proper results, led to suspicion that the extreme
values were caused by the significant difference in precision between the measuring equipment.
This was due to G being defined by the difference in τ over the difference in γ. Therefore, a 4 kN
internal force transducer was installed, which had significantly higher precision than the external
25 kN force transducer. The installation of the 4 kN force transducer significantly improved the
test results. Therefore, it was concluded that to obtain proper shear degradation curves, it was
not sufficient to only use high precision strain measurements. High precision force measurements
should also be obtained, otherwise an increase in erroneous calculations will occur, especially in
the small strain ranges.
5.2.2
Shear modulus obtained from resonant column test
The shear degradation curves obtained from the RC tests were used to cover the small strain range
of the full shear degradation curves. The RC shear degradation curves were used for comparison of
BE and local strain shear degradation curves. The shear degradation curve in the RC tests yielded
results which match the theory of the RC test. In all tests, the RC shear degradation curves showed
higher G0 values than the corresponding BE tests. This was due to several factors, which will be
discussed in more detail in the following sections.
115
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:126==
5.2.3
Shear modulus obtained from local strain LVDTs
In general the shear degradation curves obtained from local strain LVDTs showed a behavior sim-
ilar to that of the RC and DSS tests, with some deviation. This deviation was expected, since the
CID, RC and DSS tests were conducted under different boundary conditions. The CID tests were
fixed at both ends, while the RC tests were fixed at the bottom and free at the top. The DSS tests
were fixed at the top and bottom, and was forced to shear in the horizontal plane by using sliding
rings. Furthermore, in the RC tests, G was calculated based on the resonance frequency of the
system, while in the CID and DSS tests, G was calculated based on the stress and strains in the
specimen. The CID tests showed relatively consistent non-linear behavior during the strain range
γ ∈[10−2 %, 101 %]. However, in the range γ ∈[10−3 %, 10−2 %], large deviations were ob-
served. The G would either be significantly overestimated compared to the RC tests, or otherwise
it would be underestimated and slowly build up until γ ≈10−2 % was reached. Similar behav-
ior was observed by Carotenuto et al. (2023), where local strain LVDTs were used on CAD sand
specimens, and G was compared to a corresponding cyclic test. The hypothesis developed in this
research was that G found in the cyclic tests was an unloading/reloading modulus. Therefore, G
found for the corresponding static triaxial tests would be different. However, another hypothesis
could be mismatches in measurement readings. As it was observed that using the external force
transducer yielded a higher deviation, it may also be the case that, at very small strains, the internal
force transducer might still not be sufficiently accurate, for precise calculations of G.
From these results, it was not possible to estimate reliable G0 values from the local LVDTs, as the
linear soil behavior stops at much lower strains than the intermediate strain range. However, it was
possible to cover the strain range between the RC tests and the DSS tests, as shown in Figure 2.1.
5.2.4
Shear modulus obtained from direct simple shear test
DSS tests were conducted only for Dr = 80 % due to time constraints and therefore data was
limited. Dr = 80 % was chosen, due to it being a midpoint between Dr = 70 % and Dr =
90 %. Compared to the corresponding CID tests in Figure 4.2, the G obtained from the DSS
tests were lower in the strain range γ ∈[10−1 %, 100 %]. A deviation was expected between
the shear degradation curves because they were sheared differently. The CID tests were sheared
by increasing the axial displacement, allowing the sand to develop a shear plane, whereas the
DSS tests were displaced horizontally with shear rings, forcing a shear plane. The DSS test stress
state was also not fully described, as the horizontal stress was only measured in one direction,
further increasing the uncertainties. Furthermore, the use of pins on the top and bottom of the
specimen may also introduce uncertainties. In a study by Le Meil et al. (2016), it was suggested
that the embedding of pins may create uneven stress concentrations in the boundary zone. Thereby,
local softening occurs and the uniform simple shear conditions cannot be maintained. This effect
may also be present in sands. However, it seems that, as the strains increased, the CID and DSS
degradation curves converge.
5.3
Bender element
5.3.1
Comparison of interpretation methods
The G0 and Vs were determined using the FA, PTP, CC and FE interpretation methods. These
methods were compared with each other to assess which methods yielded the most reliable and
repeatable results. The RC tests were assumed to represent the correct G0 and Vs values, and
therefore these were used as a reference for the BE results. In subsection 4.2.3 the sinusoidal
burst signal results were summarized in Table 4.2 and plotted in Figure 4.9. Furthermore, the
average deviation between the interpreted Vs of each BE test and the corresponding RC Vs was
found, see Figure 4.10. When comparing the interpretation methods, it was observed that FA in
general showed a lower deviation from the RC results, with PTP showing a slightly higher deviation
than FA. The CC method shows similar results to the FE model, however, with a slightly smaller
116
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:127==
deviation towards the RC test. This was expected, because the FE model is based on the G0 found
by CC between the FE simulated signal in GeoHyTE and the experimentally obtained signal. In
general, the CC and FE method showed more consistent G0 interpretations, while the FA and PTP
method were more inconsistent.
A large deviation can be seen for the CC and FE methods in CID_8_rerun. The CID_8_rerun
received signal showed a high amount of drift, see Figure B.28, which may explain the high devi-
ation. Drift can, as described in subsection 3.2.1, be caused by incorrect alignment of the receiver
and transmitter BE. However, as some drift was also observed in CID_4_rerun, see Figure B.35,
the most probable cause was deterioration of the protective polyurethane layer on the BE. After the
test phase was completed, the BEs were inspected and the polyurethane appeared to have visual
deterioration, see Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Photograph of the transmitter BE posterior to testing. The dark spots at the red tip were
scratches in the protective polyurethane layer.
For the FA and PTP methods, the less consistent results may stem from the fact that these inter-
pretation methods were more subjective and dependent on the interpreter’s understanding of the
theory. The interpretations were conducted by the author of this thesis, who experienced diffi-
culties using these subjective time domain interpretation methods. The main issue of the time
domain interpretation methods was the distinction between the P- and S-wave arrival. As the BEs
cannot distinguish between the P- and S-waves, it was almost impossible to consistently and cor-
rectly distinguish between the two wave types. Furthermore, the PTP method appeared to show
higher inconsistencies than the FA method. This issue probably arises because of the way the BE
works. As explained earlier, the transmitter BE receives an electrical signal generated by a func-
tion generator, in this case a sinusoidal signal with a given amplitude and frequency. However,
it is impossible for the transmitter BE to replicate the signal’s motion due to the stiffness of the
BE, as described in subsection 2.2.4. Therefore, a higher delay may be observed between the input
signal peak amplitude and received signal peak amplitude, as when the input signal reaches the
peak amplitude, the BE is still accelerating, thereby creating a slight delay. This delay will be
further increased when reaching the receiver BE, as the BE will have to accelerate again, up until
it reaches the peak. This could also be seen from the tip to tip verification in Appendix B.1, as
the FA time was negligible, while the PTP delay was t = 25.56 µs. This delay was suspected to
increase as the frequency increases, due to the decrease in the signal period T. Shorter T yields
a faster acceleration of the input signal amplitude, however, the BE may not be able to accelerate
faster due to stiffness. This deemed the PTP method physically inaccurate and will underestimate
Vs and G0. Furthermore, the FA method was based on the first sharp interpretation, which was
117
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:128==
interpreted at the moment the received signal changed amplitude.
For the CC and FE methods in GeoHyTE, the consistent results stem from the minimal user input
required, resulting in objective and repeatable interpretations. However, it was expected that the
user had some experience or knowledge in the BE theory for choosing a relevant correlation win-
dow. A too small correlation window would force the CC to a higher G0 interpretation, while a
too large correlation window would cause the computational time to increase. The interpretation
of the arrival of the S-wave in the beginning of the GeoHyTE software only affected the compu-
tational time to reach convergence, which was discussed earlier in this thesis. In addition to the
correlation window and the initial G0 interpretation, the input parameters in GeoHyTE were based
either on the test setup or the specimen, which ruled out subjectivity. The advantage of GeoHyTE
was the use of the FE model which required minimal user judgment. Furthermore, in contrast to
the FA method, the arrival of the S-wave was clearer, as a clear distinction could be made between
the S-wave and P-wave, in the FE visualization software, see subsection 4.2.1. It was experienced
that determining the P-wave arrival from the visualization was somewhat difficult, because the dis-
placement fields were small, while the S-wave arrival was clear, due to larger displacement fields.
It was possible to scale the displacement field in the visualization software, which resulted in a
clearer visualization of the P-waves. Furthermore, utilizing the FE simulated output signal as the
one shown in Figure 4.8, the arrival of the P-wave could be seen clearer.
In conclusion, the FA method was a fast interpretation method, which with some experience and
engineering judgment can produce relatively reliable results. However, determining near-field
effects was still an issue in the method, which can significantly overestimate Vs or G0. The PTP
method was deemed inaccurate, due to the physical properties of the BEs, which decreased the Vs
compared to the FA method. The CC method in GeoHyTE was found to have consistent results,
with minimal user interpretation. Finally, the FE method was found to be almost as reliable as
the CC method. The advantage of the FE method was that it was possible to see not only the
S-wave arrival but also the P-wave arrival visually. The disadvantage of the FE model was the
computational time. However, it was experienced that after defining and saving the mesh, the
initial time to define the input parameters was insignificant. Thus, leaving only computational
time as an issue. This is further discussed in subsection 5.3.9. Therefore, GeoHyTE in general
was the preferred interpretation method for consistent and reliable results and to understand the
propagation of waves in the soil.
5.3.2
Comparison of bender element and resonant column results
For the sinusoidal burst signals, using the FA, PTP, CC and FE interpretation methods, the average
deviation from the corresponding RC tests was found to be between 13 % and 30 %, see Figure 4.10.
Most of the tests deviated about 20 % from the RC test, where outliers were found for CID_8_rerun
and CID_1_rerun. This suggests that on average, the Vs found in sinusoidal BE signals will deviate
around 20 % from Vs found in the RC tests. Deviations from RC were also observed by Camacho-
Tauta et al. (2013), where non-plastic soils deviated. Using the FA method, Camacho-Tauta et al.
(2013) was able to show a deviation of about 5 %, when using the Ltt travel distance. However,
it seemed as if the near-field effects were not properly assessed in this study. Camacho-Tauta
et al. (2013) also used frequency domain interpretation methods, which deviated by about 20 %,
similar to the results in this thesis. Furthermore, specimens of lower heights were used, thereby
lowering the effect of wave attenuation slightly compared to this thesis, which could also decrease
the deviation.
When Equation 3.22 was applied to the interpreted Vs, the deviation from the RC G0 could be
analyzed, see Figure 4.16. Here, the standard deviation and the average deviation were increased
compared to Vs. This was due to the fact that Vs was squared, thereby squaring the error, as well as
uncertainties from ρ. These uncertainties may stem from ρ being calculated by assuming Sr = 100
118
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:129==
%, combined with the assumption that all material was retained in the tests. These assumptions
were impossible to obtain in practice, thereby introducing deviations. The deviation was between
24 % and 50 % when comparing G0, with an average deviation of approximately 40 %. Therefore,
the deviation was increased by around 100 %.
5.3.3
Comparison of signal types
For comparison of the different signal types, the interpretation methods in this thesis were applied
to the sinusoidal, ramp, and square burst signals. For each test and signal type, the average and
standard deviation of the percentile deviation between the BE and the corresponding RC test were
calculated for all interpretation methods. The CC and FE methods were not conducted on the ramp
and square signal types due to time constraints. Therefore, the statistical parameters for these were
calculated for two data points. For future testing, it would be desirable to obtain at least three
data points for each test, for a proper statistical comparison of the signal types. The CC and FE
methods were used in one test for the ramp and square burst signals, however, the Vs yielded values
higher than the FA and PTP method. As Vs was previously slightly lower than FA and PTP in the
sinusoidal burst signals, it could be interesting in future tests to assess whether this phenomenon is
consistent. The reason for the increased values may be explained by the ramp and square signals
containing instantaneous motion. Thereby, the BE was simulated to move instantaneously, which
increased the S-wave velocity.
In Figures 4.10, 4.12 and 4.14 the average deviation between BE Vs and the corresponding RC Vs
was plotted, as well as the standard deviation for each signal type. The same plots for G0 can be
seen in Figures 4.16, 4.18 and 4.20. It was observed that the sinusoidal and square burst signals
had similar average deviation to the RC tests. The square burst signals showed a slightly lower
deviation from the corresponding RC tests than the sinusoidal burst signal. For the ramp burst
signals, the average deviation was inconsistent, with values scattered between 10 % and 34 %
deviation from the RC tests. From the ramp burst signals, it was difficult to distinguish the near-
field effects from the S-wave arrival. This was because the P-wave arrival was close to the S-wave
arrival, and thus no clear distinction could be found. This may explain the inconsistent results.
The inconsistency increased when comparing G0, because Vs was squared, thereby squaring the
error. Thus, the ramp signal was found to be the least favorable option for the interpretation of
Vs. In addition to the average deviation, the standard deviation between each of the tests was
compared. The sinusoidal burst signals were found to have smaller standard deviations than the
square burst signal. Therefore, even though the square burst signals showed a lower deviation from
the corresponding RC tests, the results contained larger uncertainties. Similarly to the ramp burst
signals, difficulties were found in distinguishing the near-field effects from the S-wave arrival.
Therefore, a larger uncertainty was introduced.
From this comparison, the sinusoidal burst signal was the most viable option, when conducting
BE measurements. This was due to several factors, including the possibility of including CC,
yielding objective interpretations of G0, which can then be compared directly to the FA method.
Furthermore, it was found to be relatively straight forward to distinguish between the compression
wave arrival and shear wave arrival, when interpreting the received signal. However, the square
burst signals, when interpreted using the FA method, could still be a viable option. Therefore,
measurements could be performed using both the sinusoidal burst signal and the square burst signal.
This would allow for the determination of an upper and lower value of G0, where the square burst
signal would act as the upper value and the sinusoidal burst signal would be the lower value.
5.3.4
Shear wave velocity at different test stages
As mentioned in subsection 3.5.2, several BE measurements were performed at different stages
of the CID tests. The resulting Vs measurements, for each test stage, can be seen in Figures 4.21
to 4.23. A clear trend was observed, where Vs decreased from installation to saturation and then in-
119
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:130==
creased at consolidation. This behavior suggests that Vs was highly dependent on the saturation of
the specimen, as only the degree of saturation changed between installation and the saturation step.
Furthermore, only the effective stress was changed from saturation to consolidation, therefore, Vs
was highly dependent on the effective stress state, which was applied to the specimen.
5.3.5
Effect of saturation
Comparison of BE measurements taken at the installation, saturation and consolidation stages of
each test was compared. The measurements were interpreted using the FA method for the sinu-
soidal burst signal. As briefly mentioned in the previous section, Vs decreased with increasing
saturation. This phenomenon was observed in previous studies by Khan et al. (2018) and Gu et
al. (2021), as described in subsection 2.4.1. The results showed a decrease in Vs after increase
in saturation, similar to the observations in this thesis. As the saturation effect on Vs seemed to
behave non-linearly in the studies, insufficient data points were obtained in this thesis to derive a
formulation to estimate saturation based on Vs and vice versa. However, the results suggested that
it might be possible to determine soil saturation, using Vs, the stress state, and Dr.
5.3.6
Effect of stress state
The S-wave velocities stated in Table 4.2 were plotted against the corresponding p′ in the test stage.
The Vs was plotted for both the FA method and the FE method, to assess correlation, but also to
ensure repeatability in the methods, see Figures 5.2 and 5.3. Reruns were not plotted since, in
general, these caused outliers due to the complications stated in section 5.1.
Figure 5.2: Vs,FA plotted against mean effective stress p′ on a logarithmic scale. A logarithmic
regression was conducted to assess correlation.
120
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:131==
Figure 5.3: The Vs,FE plotted against mean effective stress p′ on a logarithmic scale. A logarithmic
regression was conducted to assess correlation.
A clear correlation was observed in the data. The correlation was slightly reduced from the FA
method to the FE method in the tests carried out at Dr = 70 %. Besides this, the results were
similar. As observed by Abuawad et al. (2024), the shear wave velocity increased non-linearly, as
the confining stress increased. The correlation was found to be best described by a logarithmic fit.
The same plots were constructed for G0 in Table 4.5, as can be seen in Figures 5.4 and 5.5.
Figure 5.4: The G0,FA plotted against mean effective stress p′ on a logarithmic scale. A logarithmic
regression was conducted to assess correlation.
121
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:132==
Figure 5.5: The G0,FE plotted against mean effective stress p′ on a logarithmic scale. A logarithmic
regression was conducted to assess correlation.
Therefore, a high correlation was also shown between G0 and p′.
5.3.7
Comparison of travel distance interpretations
The BE interpretations were performed using both Ltt and L60 as the travel distance. From Equa-
tion 3.19 it was observed that the only difference between the two distances was that 40 % of the
BE lengths were included in L60. Therefore, the significance of the choice between the two travel
distance formulations will increase as the ratio H/Hb decreases. Therefore, the deviation from
Vs and G0 found by the two travel distances was assessed to examine the significance of the two
distances for the dimensions of the specimen used in this thesis. The average deviation from each
interpretation method can be seen in Tables 4.8 and 4.9. The average deviation of Vs found by Ltt
was found to be 2.4 % compared to L60 using the FA, PTP and CC method. However, for the FE
method, the deviation was found to be only 1.2 %. The deviation from the same comparison for G0
was found to be 4.7 % for the FA, PTP and CC method and 2.3 % for the FE method. These devia-
tions were found to be minimal, especially for the FE method. The decrease in deviation observed
from the FE method stems from the way the effective distance was calculated. The distance was
taken from the transmitter BE to the top of the specimen, as the FE model was not dependent on
the location of the receiver BE to assess the arrival of the S-waves. Therefore, as can be seen from
Equation 3.20, only the height of one BE was needed, and the dependency of εa was completely
eliminated. Thereby a significant amount of uncertainty was removed based on the specimen and
setup geometries by using the FE method.
None of the data obtained in this thesis was sufficient to conclude which of the distances was
the correct. However, it was clear that the difference between the two was insignificant for the
determination of Vs, especially when using the FE method. This was also discussed by Rio (2006),
where the conclusion was that the determination of arrival time was of more significance than the
travel distance.
5.3.8
Repeatability of bender element testing
The repeatability of the BE tests was assessed by comparing the BE measurements in the test
phases in which the density and stress states were similar. This was possible in BE measurements
performed at installation and after saturation, as these stages were similar across all tests, with only
the structure of the soil skeleton varying. Some uncertainty was expected due to changes in the
soil skeleton structure, uncertainties in the stress states caused by the pumps and the actuator, as
well as uncertainty in the interpretation methods. Thus, it was not expected to get identical results.
122
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:133==
For the installation phase, p′ was approximately 20 kPa and full saturation was not acquired. Thus,
the degree of saturation was based on the initial w of approximately 10 %. However, this w was not
accurate, as some water might evaporate or attach to the reconstitution bowls. For some tests, flow
from a de-aired water bottle was accidentally allowed into the specimen, causing an increase in
saturation. These tests were distinguishable, as they had a significant decrease in Vs at installation
compared to the corresponding tests. Under the assumption that the stress, density and saturation
states were similar in the installation phases at equal Dr, up to four BE measurements were avail-
able for each Dr to assess the repeatability of the BE measurements. The same conditions were
applied to the saturation stages, except that a higher degree of saturation was obtained. Further-
more, the soil experienced compaction at the saturation stage, because the pumps did not apply
stress at the same rate, thus increasing and decreasing the effective stress slightly. Due to this
compaction, Vs might have increased slightly.
The repeatability was assessed for each Dr by determining the mean, standard deviation, and half
range of Vs. Here, the statistical parameters were found by Equations 3.67 to 3.69, respectively.
These statistical parameters for each Dr at installation pressure can be seen in Table 4.12. In these
data, some outliers were kept out of the data. CID_1_rerun and CID_4_rerun was left out, since
these used a different test setup, compared to the original tests, as the internal force transducer was
introduced. Furthermore, CID_9 was also left out, as the de-aired water bottle was allowed to flush
water into the specimen. In the data, this was observed as a significant decrease in Vs compared
to the other test results, see Figure 4.21. In the remaining data, the highest mean Vs was found
at Dr =90 %, which was expected due to the higher density, thereby increasing Vs. However,
Dr = 70 % and Dr = 80 % show a similar mean Vs, with Dr = 70 % being slightly larger
than Dr = 80 %. This was not an expected result, as an increase in Dr should also increase Vs.
However, as mentioned earlier, the introduction of the internal force transducer may have caused
deviation as the system was changed. It was also observed that after implementation of the internal
force transducer, εa seemed to increase significantly throughout the test compared to previous tests.
This suggested that the specimen compacted more, after introducing the internal force transducer,
and thus Vs was also increased. Looking at the data, it was observed that the strain increased
significantly during the application of back pressure, compared to previous tests. Therefore, this
might also be the case for the installation pressure. This deviation may occur because a correction
for the piston rod was applied to the external force transducer, where as the internal force transducer
measures the stress directly above the specimen, thereby no correction was made. The difference in
the way stress was applied and measured can cause a deviation in the stress state that was significant
enough to introduce deviations in the test results.
The same statistical analysis was carried out at the saturation stages, see Table 4.13. Here, CID_9
was introduced in the analysis, as deviations from initial saturation was eliminated, as the specimen
was fully saturated at this stage. The standard deviation and half range were relatively constant
and low in all specimens. However, it was observed that Dr = 70 % showed a larger Vs than both
tests conducted at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90 %. As for the installation phase, this might be due to
the change of system to an internal force transducer. However, this time it had a greater effect on
Vs. This was probably due to the difference in pump rates, which caused a higher effective stress
on the specimen, thereby compacting it more.
Finally, a comparison of the reruns was made in the consolidation stages. As consolidation mea-
surements under repeated conditions were only performed for tests that contained a rerun, limited
data were available for this analysis. It was observed that after applying confining pressures, the
Vs values for the reruns were close to those of the original tests. The pumps never caused an ef-
fective stress above the consolidation stress when back pressure was increased. Therefore, when
the consolidation stress was applied, the specimens were fully compacted until EOP was reached.
Consequently, the uncertainty of the initial compaction and the increase in back pressure was re-
123
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:134==
moved. This suggested that the repeatability assessment should be conducted in the consolidation
phase, as most of the uncertainty was removed. Otherwise, saturation BE measurement should
be performed prior to increasing the back pressure. The standard deviations in the consolidation
phases were for the most part lower than those in the previous test phases, while the half range was
also significantly lower, see Table 4.14. It was observed that at the higher confining stresses, Vs
at Dr = 70 % was lower than for the equivalent confining stress on Dr = 80 %, which was not
the case in the installation and saturation stages. For future investigation of BE repeatability, more
data points should therefore be obtained in the consolidation phase of the specimens.
In conclusion, it was possible to repeat the BE measurements with reasonable certainty for all
tests. Furthermore, it was discovered that the internal force transducer had a significant impact on
Vs interpretation, as the compaction of the specimen increased. However, this did not influence
the repeatability of the tests as similar statistical parameters were derived for both test setups.
Some uncertainty will always be introduced, as test specimens and test setups can never be 100
% identical. The test specimen will vary from test to test due to the grain arrangement of the soil
changing when the specimen is reconstituted. Furthermore, the exact same density was impossible
to obtain, as especially fines tend to get lost in the process of reconstituting the specimen. Human
error was also expected when conducting experimental work, which introduces uncertainty. The
results obtained here show strong evidence that it was possible to repeat the BE results, when
applying the same soil conditions.
5.3.9
Computational time of the GeoHyTE models
The major disadvantage regarding the GeoHyTE software, was the computational time associated
with the FE model. The number of iterations as well as the computational time for each test can
be seen from Table 4.1. Most of the tests needed two iterations for the solution to converge, while
CID_4_rerun needed three, and CID_8_rerun needed four. This was most likely due to the received
signal containing a lot of drift, which caused difficulties in determining the highest correlation.
Therefore, signals containing significant drift should be discarded, as too much uncertainty is in-
troduced. For the remaining tests, the computational times were in the range of t ∈[961 s, 1420 s],
and the average time for these was 1130 s or approximately 19 min. The computations were per-
formed using the Apple M3 processor, with 4 performance cores and 4 efficiency cores, with a
maximum clock rate of 4.05 GHz. The computational time was also found to depend on the back-
ground processes on the computer. Most of the computations were done while conducting work
in for example Python or Excel, which caused an increase in the computational time. For lower
computational times, a coarser mesh could be used, with the cost of resolution and accuracy of
the FE model decreasing. A coarser mesh would furthermore increase the error as the elements
become larger. It was beyond the scope of this thesis to further analyze the discretization of the
GeoHyTE mesh, which was therefore suggested as an opportunity for future research.
5.4
Resonant column testing
From the RC tests, shear degradation curves in small strain ranges were obtained, as well as the
development of D. From Figures 4.24 and 4.26, the shear degradation curves can be observed for
the RC tests. The peak G, was assumed to be the representative G0 of the sand, which was used
for comparison of the BE results. As expected, the curves peaked at the very small shear strains
at γ ∈[10−4%, 10−3%], followed by a decrease in G, and the curves flattening out at around
γ = 10−1 %. In Figures 4.27 to 4.29 the damping curves can be seen for the RC tests. Here, D
was low during the small values of γ, but increased with increasing γ. The increase in D occurred
due to compaction of the soil specimen during the torsional resonance phase, as εv increased, which
can be seen in Figures D.1 to D.3 in Appendix D.
124
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:135==
5.5
Triaxial testing
5.5.1
Peak and critical state envelopes
From Figures 4.34 to 4.36, the triaxial test shear phases were plotted as τ against p′ grouped by
Dr. In the graphs, the CSL and the peak envelope were plotted, constructed by a linear regression
between the CSL and peak points, respectively. In the tests conducted at Dr = 70 %, the specimens
showed behavior of a loose sand, and therefore the peak and the CSL envelope were similar. It was
also observed that the rerun conducted at Dr = 70 % aligns almost perfectly with the original test.
This was not the case for the reruns at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90 %, as can be seen in Figures 4.32
and 4.33. This might be explained by the internal force transducer being implemented, thereby a
change in the test setup. Therefore, the reruns at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90 % were not included in
the stress path graphs. All tests conducted at Dr = 70 % were carried out with the internal force
transducer, and therefore these showed similar results. This further suggested that BE repeatability
between reruns decreased due to the change in the system.
Using the Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion (Labuz & Zang, 2012), the drained cohesion and friction
angle were determined for the triaxial tests, see Table 4.16. The tests carried out at Dr = 70 %
showed little to no cohesion, while Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90 % showed high cohesion. The
cohesion originates from the fines content in the sand (Phan et al., 2016b). The cohesion for
Dr = 80 % was found to be slightly higher than for Dr = 90 %. However, the friction angle was
observed to increase as Dr increased, except that the friction angle in the CSL for Dr = 70 % was
slightly higher than for Dr = 80 %.
5.5.2
Maximum angle of dilatancy compared to shear wave velocity
The ψmax found for each test can be seen in Table 4.17. The ψmax was not found for the tests
conducted at Dr = 70 %, as dilation was not observed. In CID_1_rerun an unloading/reloading
phase was introduced, which increased the ψmax compared to the original test and was therefore
left out, as it was not representative of the original data. When plotting ψmax as a function of
Vs using the FA method, a linear correlation appears to be present, see Figure 5.6. The same was
observed when plotting against Vs found using the FE method, however, a slightly lower inclination
of the linear regression was observed, see Figure 5.7. For the tests conducted at Dr =90 %, a rerun
was included in the data, however, this yielded approximately the same results as the original test,
which also shows potential repeatability of the results. As data were limited in this thesis, the
correlation could be further investigated in the future by conducting more tests at the same Dr and
introducing more p′ states, for a wider data range.

==PAGE:136==
Figure 5.6: The ψmax plotted as a function of BE measurements interpreted using the FA method.
Linear regression was applied to assess correlation.
Figure 5.7: The ψmax plotted as a function of BE measurements interpreted using the Finite Ele-

5.5.3
Critical state parameter compared to shear wave velocity
The Ψ was found for each test, as shown in Table 4.18. The values obtained were compared to
the general definition of Ψ stated by Reid et al. (2023) on idealized sand specimens. From this
study, Ψ < −0.05 would tend to be dilative (dense sand), Ψ ≈0.00 would be medium dense, and
Ψ > 0.05 would tend to be contractive (loose sand). The values within this thesis are within the
126
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:137==
range Ψ ∈[−0.05, 0.05], suggesting a slight deviation to the theory stated in Reid et al. (2023),
as the soil behaved loose at Dr = 70 %, and dense at Dr = 80 % and 90 %. This deviation may
be explained by the difference in the material used. In this thesis, the sand contains fines, which
might cause deviations. Furthermore, uncertainties in e and e0 could also cause these deviations,
as further described in the following.
The Ψ was plotted against Vs found by the FA and FE method in Figures 5.8 and 5.9. It was not
possible to observe a correlation for the tests conducted at Dr = 80 %, since less than three reliable
data points were available. When inspecting the stress strain curve on Figure 4.32, it was observed
that the test conducted at Dr = 80 % and p′ = 50 kPa showed soil hardening followed by a
decrease in τ. It was unknown why the soil showed this behavior, but it was suspected to be due to
development of shear planes. CID_8_rerun was not included, due to the large deviation in Vs from
the original run. Furthermore, the reruns conducted at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90% deviated from
the original run due to the implementation of the internal force transducer. However, a correlation
was still observed between Ψ and Vs. An increase in Ψ, was suggested to yield higher values of
Vs, as can be seen in Figure 5.8. Some deviation between the FA method and FE method was
observed, as the FE method tends to yield slightly lower values of Vs than FA. The inclination of
the linear regression seemed to decrease slightly at Dr = 90 %, as a result of this. Otherwise, both
methods showed correlation with Ψ. As the calculations of e were based on εv from volumetric
change in the back pressure pump, under the assumption that e0 is constant up until consolidation,
some uncertainty was introduced in the results. Furthermore, e0 was calculated, assuming perfect
reconstitution of the specimen, using Equation 3.15.
To create a reliable and robust formulation for Ψ as a function of Vs, precise measurements of
e should be obtained. Therefore, some implementations could be made to improve the results of
these tests. First, e0 could be found experimentally during saturation using a scale connected to the
triaxial setup, similar to the permeability test setup described in subsection 3.2.3. Here, the speci-
men should be reconstituted completely dry, followed by saturation using the back pressure pump.
Then excess water would be allowed to flow to the scale, measuring the amount of water which
is collected on the scale, and subtracting it from the change in volume of the back pressure pump.
Another method to ensure precise e values is by implementation of local radial strain LVDTs. This
was initially planned, however, as one of the three local LVDTs did not work properly, it was
prioritized to measure the axial local strains, and therefore local radial strain measurements were
not measured. However, from these results, a correlation between Ψ and Vs seems reasonable,
since the Ψ was dependent on e0 and eCSL. It was shown by the rerun conducted at Dr = 70
% and p′ = 100 kPa, that it was possible to repeat the CSL at the shear phase, as seen in Fig-
ure 4.31. Therefore, it was suggested that a correlation between Ψ and Vs can be found if precise
measurements are conducted.
127
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:138==
Figure 5.8: Critical State Parameter Ψ plotted as a function of BE measurements interpreted using
the FA method. Linear regression was applied to assess correlation.
Figure 5.9: Critical State Parameter Ψ plotted as a function of BE measurements interpreted using
the FE method. Linear regression was applied to assess correlation.
5.5.4
Unloading/Reloading properties
For tests conducted after CID_3, an unloading/reloading stage was introduced. From the unload-
ing/reloading phase, EUR, GUR and νUR were obtained, see Table 4.20. For CID_4 the unload-
ing/reloading stage was performed after reaching the peak deviatoric stress. Therefore, the results
128
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:139==
were not representable, as high unloading/reloading moduli were observed. For CID_6 problems
with εv were observed and therefore GUR was not obtained. For most tests, it was observed that the
secant moduli were lower than those at the unloading/reloading stage, see Tables 4.19 and 4.20. For
CID_1_rerun it was observed that the secant modulus was larger than those at unloading/reloading.
This was suspected to be due to the axial stress being accidentally increased by 100 kPa after the
consolidation stress was reached. This short application of stress likely caused the soil to harden,
causing an increase in stiffness. Therefore, the CID_1_rerun results deviated from the remaining
tests. The νUR was calculated from EUR and GUR using Equation 3.60, as a way to verify the
results. However, it was found that the results deviated significantly for most tests. When plotting
the calculated and experimentally obtained νUR, it was observed that the results seemed to follow
a 1:2 ratio, see Figure 5.10.
Figure 5.10: Calculated Poisson’s ratio at unloading νUR,calc plotted against Experimentally ob-
tained Poisson’s ratio at unloading νUR.
Two outliers were observed, one for Dr = 80 % and one for Dr = 90 %. The Dr = 80 %
outlier was suggested to be caused by the accidental load explained earlier. It was unknown why
the Dr = 90 % deviated from the remaining data. However, it may be due to the use of the external
force transducer causing inaccuracy in the measurements.
A suggestion as to why the experimental νUR showed values higher than the calculated νUR,calc,
was due to the high Sr in the soil. In a study conducted by Yan et al. (2023), it was found that for
soils with Sr close to 100 %, ν increased significantly. As the tests in this thesis were carried out
using fully saturated specimens at high back pressure, the specimens were approaching Sr = 100
%, and thus an increase in ν would be expected. Especially the tests conducted at Dr = 70 %
had a high saturation, due to the sand being loose. Another theory was that the specimen diameter
to height ratio might affect the experimental νUR as well. This theory originated by comparing
the results to a study in which similar unloading/reloading tests were conducted on sand (Latini
& Zania, 2016). However, the specimens had a different height to diameter ratio, with height and
diameter equal to 7 cm. From these results, the ratio between the experimentally obtained νUR and
the calculated νUR was significantly lower. The issue of the deviation of the data in this thesis arises
because commercial software, such as PLAXIS (Bentley Systems, 2024) utilizes EUR and νUR as
input parameters for the calculation of GUR using Equation 3.60. In the case of the test results
129
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:140==
presented in this thesis, this would significantly underestimate GUR. As a result, overestimation
of foundation design occurs, which will cause unnecessary expenses and environmental impact to
construction projects. Therefore, this observation leaves room for future research to assess whether
an updated formulation for Equation 3.60 should be derived. In the case of the results of this thesis,
a suggested reformulation could be written as Equation 5.1.
GUR =
EUR
2(1 −νUR/2)
for
Sr = 100%
(5.1)
5.5.5
Permeability testing
From the permeability tests, a representative value of κ was found for each Dr. The results can be
seen from Tables 4.21 to 4.23. The values were used for the GeoHyTE analysis, where permeability
was an input parameter. However, these results were only an estimate of the κ in the tests. In
reality, κ would deviate slightly, as different confinement stresses were applied as described by Hu
et al. (2020). The porosity decreases as the confinement stress increases and, therefore, it would be
expected that the permeability decreases as a result. As can be seen in Table 4.21, which represents
the results of Dr = 70 %, κ was significantly higher than for the remaining tests. This was probably
due to the porosity being higher, allowing the pores to transmit water more effectively. Another
reason might be that the soil κ was greater than the κ of the system. Thus, κ was measured for the
system, and not for the soil specimen. The cell pressure at Dr = 70 % was accidentally increased
by 15 kPa compared to the other two permeability tests, thereby decreasing the porosity. According
to Hu et al. (2020), this should lead to a decrease in κ, thus κ could be slightly underestimated for
Dr = 70 %, assuming that κ was representative of the soil and not the system as explained above.
Comparison of GeoHyTE results conducted at Dr = 70 % and the remaining tests leads to the
conclusion that κ did not significantly impact the results. Therefore, κ was assumed to be correct,
as time was insufficient for a rerun. The tests conducted at Dr = 80 % and Dr = 90 % showed
similar results when compared, as can be seen from Tables 4.22 and 4.23. The κ for Dr = 90 %
was slightly higher than for Dr = 80 %. This was unexpected, since the porosity was lower for
Dr = 90 %. Therefore, the arrangement of the soil grains might have allowed a slightly better
flow in the test conducted at Dr = 90 %.
5.6
Direct simple shear testing
The DSS tests were performed to cover the large strain range γ ∈[100 %, 102 %] in the shear
degradation curves. A new saturation approach was applied for the DSS tests, and the results were
compared with the conventional saturation approach.
5.6.1
A new saturation approach
As the original DSS tests using the conventional way of saturating the specimen yielded stress-
strain curves which were not representative of the triaxial tests, a new saturation method was as-
sessed. This method was inspired by Reid et al. (2024), where a different approach to saturation
was carried out on silt specimens. The results were stress-strain curves that aligned better with the
corresponding triaxial tests. Therefore, a similar approach was used in this thesis. However, the
tests described by Reid et al. (2024) were carried out with constant back pressure applied to the
specimen, throughout the test. This was not possible to replicate in this thesis because the water
container was open at the top in the DSS setup. The resulting stress-strain curves were plotted with
the DSS tests using the conventional saturation method in Figure 4.37. It was observed that as the
confining stress increased, the difference between the conventional method and the new method
decreased. For the confining stress of p′ = 200 kPa, the CSL was slightly lower, whereas for
p′ = 50 kPa and p′ = 100 kPa a significant difference was observed. From Figure 4.38 clearer
degradation paths were observed, using the new saturation method. This suggested that the origi-
nal saturation method does not allow the specimen to fully saturate, as the water pressure was not
130
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:141==
sufficient to flush all of the pores in the specimen. By this new procedure, it was ensured that air
was flushed out of the test setup, prior to flushing the specimen with de-aired tap water.
5.6.2
Shear strength
As can be seen in Figure 4.39, no cohesion was found from the DSS tests. The friction angle
for the peak envelope was found to be φ = 27.0°, while for the CSL envelope it was found to
be φ = 26.1, as stated in Table 4.24. The friction angle for the DSS tests was therefore lower
than those for the corresponding triaxial tests. The same results were seen in previous research by
Atkinson et al. (1991), where the friction angle in DSS tests were lower than in the corresponding
triaxial tests. It was suggested that this behavior was caused by the stress state in the DSS tests not
being completely defined. The normal stress and shear stress in the horizontal plane was measured,
while in the triaxial test the stress state was well defined on all sides of the specimen.
5.7
Future work
Considering the results and limitations described in this thesis, future work for further investigation
will be described in this section.
5.7.1
Improved shear degradation curves at intermediate strain range
In section 5.2 it was found that changing the test setup to use an internal force transducer yielded a
significant improvement in the shear degradation curves in the intermediate strain range. However,
in the strain ranges γ ∈[10−3 %, 10−2 %], large deviations were still observed. Therefore, it
was of interest to repeat the triaxial tests using an even more precise force transducer. However,
this would make it almost impossible to conduct the full shear phase, as the forces would most
likely exceed the force transducer capacity. Therefore, the specimen could be sheared in the range
γ ∈[10−3 %, 100 %]. The DSS tests could be used for the strains γ ≤100 %, thus still obtaining
the full degradation curve.
5.7.2
Repetition of shear degradation curves
For future testing, the repeatability of shear degradation curves could be researched. In this the-
sis, it was shown that it was possible to obtain repeatable CSL values in triaxial tests, as seen in
Figure 4.31. Furthermore, BE tests were shown to be repeatable as seen in Tables 4.12 to 4.14.
Therefore, repeating all test types conducted at the same Dr and p′ could show if the shear degra-
dation curves are repeatable.
5.7.3
Interpretation of sine sweep signal
As time was insufficient, the measurements of the sine sweep signal were not interpreted. The
signals were expected to be interpreted using the cross-spectrum method stated in Equation 2.3.
Furthermore, it was desired to assess whether the time domain methods could be used for reliable
values of Vs using a sine sweep signal. This was of interest, as the procedure of looking for the
resonant frequency was time consuming. Therefore, if it were possible to find a way to determine
Vs in the time domain using a fine sweep, much time could be saved for future BE testing.
5.7.4
Resonant column testing with bender elements
In subsection 5.3.2 the Vs found in the RC tests was compared to those found by the BE tests.
The BE tests deviated by approximately 20 % from the corresponding RC tests. As the boundary
conditions of the two test setups were different, it would be of interest to conduct BE measurements
in the RC tests as well. Thus, the boundary conditions as well as the soil properties would be the
same, ruling out uncertainties that may arise from this.
5.7.5
Assessment of wave attenuation
Another explanation for the BE tests deviating from the RC tests was that the wave attenuation
yielded lower Vs estimates, as the S-waves were slowed by the soil. Therefore, tests further inves-
tigating this could be conducted. This can be obtained by adjusting the specimen height and thereby
131
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:142==
assessing the deviation. Another method could be measuring the D of the BE signal, to assess how
much the signal dampens, after reaching the peak amplitude, thus estimating the influence of wave
attenuation.
5.7.6
Correlation between degree of saturation and shear wave velocity
In subsection 5.3.5 it was assessed that a decrease in Vs was correlated with an increase in saturation
of the sand. However, since previous studies (Gu et al., 2021) showed non-linear changes in Vs as
saturation increased, the test data presented in this thesis were insufficient to show a formulation
of the correlation. Therefore, the same tests could be conducted, with a focus on saturation. The
sand could be reconstituted completely dry, followed by BE measurements while incrementally
increasing the Skempton B value of the specimen. Thus, more data points are collected to perform
a proper formulation.
5.7.7
Correlation between mean effective stress and shear wave velocity
In subsection 5.3.6 a high correlation was found between p′ and Vs. As only three Dr and p′
were performed, the data was limited. Therefore, for a further repeatability assessment of this
correlation, more tests could be performed at different values of p′ and Dr.
5.7.8
Repeatability of bender element testing
In subsection 5.3.8 it was possible to show repeatability of BE interpretations at the installation and
saturation stages of the tests. However, the consolidation stages had unique values of Dr and p′ for
every test, except for the reruns. Therefore, it was not possible to properly assess repeatability in
consolidation, as a maximum of two equal density and stress states were obtainable. Furthermore,
as the system was changed, repeatability was also affected. Therefore, future tests could repeat the
same test, with the same test setup, to properly assess the repeatability of the consolidation phase.
Furthermore, this could include the repeatability of the GeoHyTE interpretation.
5.7.9
GeoHyTE using different waveforms
As time was limited, the ramp and square burst signals were not interpreted for all tests in this
thesis. In subsection 4.2.3 Vs for one test was found using GeoHyTE on all signal types. The value
of Vs was found to be greater than that of FA and PTP in the ramp and square burst signals, which
was not observed for sinusoidal burst signals. Therefore, future research could assess whether this
phenomenon is repeatable.
5.7.10
Discretization of GeoHyTE mesh
As discussed in subsection 5.3.9, the disadvantage of GeoHyTE was the computational time. The
computational time can be reduced by using a coarser mesh or decreasing the algorithmic properties
stated in Table 3.8 of the GeoHyTE model. The consequence of this would be an increased error
as well as a decrease in the resolution of the FE visualization. Therefore, a benchmark model with
a very fine mesh could be utilized, followed by models with a decreased refinement of the mesh
and algorithmic properties. For each model, the deviation from the benchmark model could be
assessed, thereby assessing the increase in error with a decrease in accuracy.
5.7.11
Correlation between maximum angle of dilatancy and shear wave velocity
In subsection 5.5.2 a correlation was found between ψmax and Vs. However, limited data were
available because a maximum of four data points were available for the evaluation of a correlation.
Therefore, for future investigations, similar tests could be conducted, but at a larger number of
tests. As ψmax depends on εa and εv, local radial and axial strain measurements would be ideal for
a precise measurement of the change in strain. The same setup could be used to obtain improved
measurements of e as explained in subsection 5.7.12.
5.7.12
Correlation between critical state parameter and shear wave velocity
In subsection 5.5.3 a correlation was found between Ψ and Vs. However, very limited data was
available since a maximum of four data points were available for the correlation assessment. Fur-
132
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:143==
thermore, the tests were conducted assuming that there was no change in e until consolidation,
which was fundamentally wrong. Therefore, for future investigation, similar tests could be con-
ducted, however, using local radial and axial strain measurements. This would result in precise
measurements of εr, thus improving the precision of e.
5.7.13
Poisson’s ratio at unloading
A deviation was observed between νUR obtained experimentally, and νUR calculated from Equa-
tion 3.60 using theory of elasticity, see subsection 5.5.4. This deviation can be further assessed
by conducting more experiments, using local axial and radial strain measurements, to increase the
accuracy of determining νUR.
133
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:144==
6
Conclusions
The scope of this thesis was to investigate methods for obtaining full shear degradation modulus
curves in sand. An extensive laboratory program was conducted on sand, to obtain G values from
bender elements, resonant column, local strain in triaxial testing, as well as direct simple shear
tests.
6.1
Reliable shear degradation curves
Full shear degradation curves were obtained, combining the shear degradation curves of individual
tests. The shear degradation curves showed some deviation between the tests, which was expected
due to the different boundary conditions of each test. However, a similar behavior was observed.
It was found that especially equipment precision was important for obtaining reliable shear degra-
dation curves. In the triaxial tests, an external force transducer was used for half of the tests, which
caused unreliable shear degradation curves. After the implementation of an internal force trans-
ducer, which had higher precision, the shear degradation curves improved significantly. As time
was limited, it was not possible to repeat tests, and thereby assess the repeatability of the shear
degradation curves.
6.2
Improved assessment of wave arrival in bender element testing
A major issue of the bender element test is the amount of uncertainty involved in interpreting the
results. In this thesis, four different interpretation methods were examined, namely the first arrival,
peak to peak, cross-correlation (in GeoHyTE), and finite element method (in GeoHyTE). Bender
element tests were known to tend to deviate from the reference tests due to several factors in the soil.
Therefore, not only was the goal to obtain BE interpretations close to the corresponding resonant
column tests, they had to be reliable and repeatable. In addition, the ease of use was also assessed.
It was found that using the conventional time domain interpretation methods, first arrival and peak
to peak, yielded quick and reliable interpretations. However, issues distinguishing the near-field
effects in the first arrival method were observed. Furthermore, the peak to peak method tended to
show lower values of shear wave velocity Vs, than the first arrival method. Therefore, the Matlab
based GeoHyTE software was researched. GeoHyTE required little user interpretation to obtain
the Small Strain Shear Modulus G0 which resulted in objective and reliable values of G0. The
model contains a visualization of the bender element test, showing the wave propagation through
the specimen. Thus, the interpreter can see the compression waves and shear waves propagating
in the soil. This allowed for a clear distinction between the near-field effects and the shear wave
arrival, which solves one of the biggest issues in bender element testing.
6.3
Optimal waveforms
Burst signals were used throughout the analysis of the thesis. Here, a sinusoidal, ramp, and square
signal was used. However, using the first arrival method, it was found that the square burst signal
showed the lowest deviation from the corresponding resonant column tests, with a higher standard
deviation than the sinusoidal signal. The ramp burst signal had the highest deviation from the
corresponding resonant column tests, as well as the highest standard deviation. Furthermore, the
ramp and square burst signals contained instantaneous motion, which the bender elements are not
capable of replicating. Therefore, it was concluded that the sinusoidal burst signal was the most
reliable signal type.
134
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:145==
6.4
Correlation between saturation and shear wave arrival
Bender element measurements conducted before and after saturation of the sand specimen, sug-
gested that a correlation was present between Vs and the degree of saturation. For all tests, after
saturation of the specimen, Vs decreased. Prior studies showed that the effect of saturation was
non-linear, and therefore, insufficient data points were available for establishing a formulation of
the correlation.
6.5
Repeatability of bender element tests
A repeatability assessment of the results of the bender element tests was performed. The Vs was
found to show a low standard deviation and half range. The repeatability was assessed at three
different test stages, including the installation phase, saturation phase, and after consolidation. It
was found that at all stages the standard deviation and half range were relatively low, while at the
consolidation stage they were the lowest. This proved that the bender element tests were repeatable
with an insignificant amount of uncertainty.
6.6
Correlation between the maximum angle of dilatancy and shear
wave velocity
From the triaxial tests, the maximum angle of dilatancy ψmax was determined for the shear phases
of the dilative soils. It was suggested that a correlation was present between ψmax and Vs because
Vs is dependent on the pore structure of the soil specimen. When plotting ψmax against Vs, a
linear correlation was found to be present. Data in this thesis were limited and therefore a robust
formulation for the correlation was not possible. This left room for future testing.
6.7
Ranges for the critical state parameter in silty sands
In subsection 5.5.3 the Ψ obtained from this thesis was compared to a study by Reid et al. (2023).
A deviation in Ψ was found in this thesis, which was suggested to be due to the fines content of the
BKR02 sand. Therefore, future research could investigate the behavior of silty sand, in relation to
the study conducted by Reid et al. (2023).
6.8
Correlation between the critical state parameter and shear wave
velocity
From the triaxial tests, the critical state parameter Ψ was determined for all tests. As with ψmax,
a correlation between Ψ and Vs was suggested, because Ψ is dependent on the void ratio and thus
the pore structure of the specimen. When plotting Ψ against Vs, a linear correlation was found to
be present. Data in this thesis were limited, and therefore a robust formulation for the correlation
was not possible. This left room for future testing.
6.9
Deviation from elastic theory in unloading/reloading phases
Unloading/reloading shear phases were performed for most of the triaxial tests. Here the Young’s
modulus EUR, shear modulus GUR, and Poisson’s ratio νUR at unloading were determined. The
experimentally obtained νUR was found to be significantly higher than when calculated from EUR
and GUR based on the theory of elasticity. This behavior was suspected to be due to the high satu-
ration in the sand, which in previous studies had been shown to significantly increase ν. Therefore,
future research could focus on this behavior to assess whether a reformulation could be derived.

B
Bender element interpretations
B.1
Verification of bender elements
The verification of the bender elements used in this thesis using the tip-to-tip measurement can be
seen in Figure B.1.
Figure B.1: Tip-to-tip verification of bender elements.
As can be seen, no delay was found using the FA interpretation method, where a slight delay of
t = 25.56µs was found for the PTP interpretation method. This delay was found to be negligible
and, therefore, not accounted for.

Notes: *Bender Element measurements are done for different frequencies from 1 kHz to 15 kHz, until the 
resonance frequency is found. The resonance frequency measurement is saved, as well as the resonance 
frequency ±0.5 kHz. Then measurements are repeated with different signal shape (Ramp wave and Square

104 # Calculating bulk density by assuming 100% saturation
105 def Calculate_rho(rho_dry):
106
Sr = 100
107
w = -(Sr * (-rho_s + rho_dry)) / (rho_s * rho_dry)
108

197 #Define a function to find the consolidation stress for graph axis limits
198 def find_cons_stress(value,targets=[50,100,200]):
199
closest = min(targets ,key=lambda x: abs(value-x))
200
return closest
201
202 #Define a function to round up to closest 50 for graph axis limits
203 def nearest_50(value):
204
return (math.ceil(value/50))*50
205
206 #Define a function to round up to closest 5 for graph axis limits
207 def nearest_5(value):
208
return (math.ceil(value/5))*5
209
210 #Define a function to round down and up to closest 0.01 for graph axis limits
211 def nearest_01(value):
212
x1 = (math.floor(value/0.01))*0.01
213
x2 = (math.ceil(value/0.01))*0.01
214
return [x1,x2]
215
216 #Define a function to round up to closest 0.5
217 def nearest_05(value):
218
return(math.ceil(value/0.5))*0.5

Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:403==
142 #Phase 1 ------------
143 Delta_t1 = Time[Phase1_index]-Time[Phase1_index[0]]
144 Ubot1 = data_calc[Phase1_index ,7]
145 Mean_temp1 = np.mean(Temp[Phase1_index]) #[oC]
146 Time_phase1 = Delta_t1[-1] #[min]
147 Delta_Weight1 = Weight[Phase1_index]-Weight[Phase1_index[0]]
148 rho_w1 = np.interp(Mean_temp1 ,water_density[:,0],water_density[:,1])
149 outflow1 = Delta_Weight1 / rho_w1 #cm3
150 inflow1 = np.abs(Utop_V[Phase1_index]-Utop_V[Phase1_index[0]])
151 Q1 = (outflow1[-1]/1000000)/(Time_phase1*60) #m3/s
152 Mean_Ubot1 = np.mean(Ubot1) #kPa
153 h1 = mH2O*Mean_Ubot1
154 kT1 = (Q1*H0/100)/(h1*A0/10000)
155
156 #Phase 2 ------------
157 Delta_t2 = Time[Phase2_index]-Time[Phase2_index[0]]
158 Ubot2 = data_calc[Phase2_index ,7]

160 Time_phase2 = Delta_t2[-1] #[min]
161 Delta_Weight2 = Weight[Phase2_index]-Weight[Phase2_index[0]]
162 rho_w2 = np.interp(Mean_temp2 ,water_density[:,0],water_density[:,1])
163 outflow2 = Delta_Weight2 / rho_w2
164 inflow2 = np.abs(Utop_V[Phase2_index]-Utop_V[Phase2_index[0]])
165 Q2 = (outflow2[-1]/1000000)/(Time_phase2*60) #m3/s
166 Mean_Ubot2 = np.mean(Ubot2) #kPa
167 h2 = mH2O*Mean_Ubot2
168 kT2 = (Q2*H0/100)/(h2*A0/10000)
169
170 #Phase 3 ------------
171 Delta_t3 = Time[Phase3_index]-Time[Phase3_index[0]]
172 Ubot3 = data_calc[Phase3_index ,7]
173 Mean_temp3 = np.mean(Temp[Phase3_index]) #[oC]
174 Time_phase3 = Delta_t3[-1] #[min]
175 Delta_Weight3 = Weight[Phase3_index]-Weight[Phase3_index[0]]
176 rho_w3 = np.interp(Mean_temp3 ,water_density[:,0],water_density[:,1])
177 outflow3 = Delta_Weight3 / rho_w3
178 inflow3 = np.abs(Utop_V[Phase3_index]-Utop_V[Phase3_index[0]])
179 Q3 = (outflow3[-1]/1000000)/(Time_phase3*60) #m3/s
180 Mean_Ubot3 = np.mean(Ubot3) #kPa

63 def analyse_file(filename):
64
#Find testname from filename
65
testname_file = filename.split('/')[2].split('.')[0]
66
testname = testname_code.get(testname_file)
67
#Initialize data storage
68
data = {}
69
current_phase = None
70
current_step = None
71
Shear = []
72
73
#Load data
74
with open(filename ,'r') as file:
75
for line in file:
76
#Check phase
77
if line.startswith("Phase:"):
78
current_phase = line.split(":")[1].strip()
79
elif line.startswith("Step:"):
80
current_step = line.split(":")[1].strip()
81
82
#Create key for current phase and step
83
if current_phase == "1":
84
current_key = f"Consolidation_Step_{current_step}"
85
elif current_phase == "2" and current_step == "1":
86
current_key = "Shear"
87
else:
88
current_key = f"Phase_{current_phase}_Step_{current_step}"
89
data[current_key] = []
90
elif current_phase and current_step and line.strip():
91
try:
92
row = [float(val) for val in line.split()]
397
Advanced Characterization of Shear Modulus Reduction from Small to Large Strains in Sand
==PAGE:408==
93
data[current_key].append(row)
94
except ValueError:
95
#Skip non-numeric lines
96
pass
97
#Convert to Pandas datafram
98
data = {key: pd.DataFrame(value) for key, value in data.items() if value}
99
100
#Remove phases with "None"
101
data = {key: df for key, df in data.items() if 'None' not in key}
102
103
#Remove columns header
104
for key in data:
105
data[key] = data[key].iloc[:].reset_index(drop=True)
106
107
return testname_file , testname , data
